## Assistant (2026-04-25 20:54:32)

_Thought for 11s_

# Agent 2 — Reduced-Base Adversary

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{I do not find a structural mechanism that traps a nondegenerate off-line packet entirely inside }
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}\cup\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core,7}.
}
\]

The strongest adversarial conclusion is weaker:

\[
\boxed{
\text{reduced-base reach is conditionally valid, but still needs a global non-swallowing theorem for same-scale/core-gap/Gram walls.}
}
\]

The walls

\[
x_m=0,\qquad q^{(7)}(m)=0,\qquad q''(m)=0,\qquad g=h,\qquad y=0
\]

are proper analytic conditions for a nondegenerate off-line packet. They cannot swallow a local midpoint neighbourhood unless the midpoint is artificially fixed or a new identity is imposed. The serious remaining risks are the broader reduced-base walls:

\[
\text{same-scale eligibility},\qquad
\text{core-gap failure},\qquad
\det G_{\core}=0,
\]

because these depend on the surrounding zero configuration and not only on the single packet. The attached handover correctly identifies this as Agent 2’s mission: try to break reduced-base reach rather than restate its definition. 

---

## 1. Visibility walls do not swallow the packet

Let

\[
\rho=\beta+i\gamma,\qquad 0<\beta<1,\qquad \beta\ne\frac12,
\]

and set

\[
\delta=\beta-\frac12,\qquad
a=\frac12-\delta,\qquad
b=\frac12+\delta,\qquad
x_m=2m-\gamma.
\]

For the paired packet contribution,

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2}+\frac{b}{b^2+x^2},
\qquad x=2t-\gamma,
\]

we have near the centred point

\[
q^{(7)}(m)
=
5160960(a^{-9}+b^{-9})x_m+O(x_m^3).
\]

Since \(a,b>0\),

\[
a^{-9}+b^{-9}>0.
\]

Therefore

\[
\boxed{x_m=0\text{ is a simple zero of }q^{(7)}(m).}
\]

So the wall

\[
q^{(7)}=0
\]

cannot contain a neighbourhood of admissible \(m\). A small non-centred perturbation activates the \(K_1\)-visibility channel.

Similarly, at the centred packet,

\[
q''(m)=2A_2=-8(a^{-3}+b^{-3})\ne0.
\]

Thus the wall

\[
q''=0
\]

does not swallow the local packet family unless other background/core contributions are tuned to cancel the packet curvature identically. That would be a new analytic incidence condition, not a symmetry consequence.

For the one-whitening-slot centred route, the remaining Gram-isotropy wall is

\[
g=h,
\]

where

\[
g=\frac{q_0}{\pi},\qquad
h=\frac1\pi\left(\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''\right).
\]

This is equivalent to

\[
q''=6q_0-2q_0^3.
\]

For the clean packet example

\[
\delta=\frac14,\qquad q_0=\frac{16}{3},\qquad q''=-\frac{14336}{27},
\]

while

\[
6q_0-2q_0^3=-\frac{7328}{27}.
\]

So

\[
q''\ne6q_0-2q_0^3.
\]

Hence \(g=h\) is not structurally forced by packet symmetry.

Finally, the mixed-block coefficient satisfies at leading order

\[
y_{\rm lead}=-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72}.
\]

On a good patch \(q_0\ne0\), and for an off-line packet \(A_2\ne0\), so

\[
\boxed{y_{\rm lead}\ne0.}
\]

Therefore the visibility walls

\[
x_m=0,\quad q^{(7)}=0,\quad q''=0,\quad g=h,\quad y=0
\]

are proper analytic walls, not open swallowing mechanisms.

---

## 2. Endpoint rigidity is not structural

The local construction uses

\[
t_\pm=m\pm\frac{s}{2}.
\]

Thus \(m\) is a sampling midpoint, not intrinsically the packet centre. If the proof artificially imposes

\[
m=\gamma/2,
\]

then the \(q^{(7)}\)-visibility channel is killed by parity and one must use the centred \(A_9\) fallback. But that is a self-imposed gauge restriction, not a necessity of the finite-\(s\) local block.

The adversarial question is whether moving \(m\) off-centre breaks the rest of the reduced-base conditions. It does not, provided the starting point lies in the interior of the reduced good region. Strict inequalities persist under small perturbation:

\[
D_{P_k}(m)<D_{P_{k+1}}(m),
\]

same-scale bounds, endpoint separation, and Gram nondegeneracy. Thus endpoint rigidity is not a kill switch unless the draft later restricts to the centred slice.

---

## 3. Core-gap failure can be controlled locally, but global reach remains open

The packet-level core-gap condition is

\[
D_{P_k}(m)<D_{P_{k+1}}(m).
\]

The failure wall is

\[
D_{P_k}(m)=D_{P_{k+1}}(m).
\]

On a fixed labelled packet stratum, the packet score functions \(D_P(m)\) are analytic or semianalytic. Therefore the tie wall is controlled.

The adversarial possibility is stronger:

\[
D_{P_k}(m)=D_{P_{k+1}}(m)
\quad\text{for all admissible }m
\]

near the packet.

This would require either:

1. an identically tied competing packet score;
2. a symmetry-shell cut, where \(k\) cuts through a forced equality shell;
3. a rigid midpoint choice that lands on the tie wall.

Packet-level ordering removes internal symmetry ties, so case 2 is avoided if \(k\) counts symmetry packets rather than individual zeros. Case 3 is avoided by midpoint freedom. Case 1 is the only real adversarial risk: a distinct competing packet with an identically equal score over the admissible interval. That would mean an additional geometric symmetry or degeneracy between distinct packets, and should be placed in a controlled exceptional stratum.

So the core-gap conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Core-gap failure is locally controlled, but global non-swallowing depends on excluding identically tied distinct packets.}
}
\]

This is a real dependency, but not evidence against the rank-two route.

---

## 4. Same-scale eligibility is the most serious reach risk

Same-scale eligibility is less purely local than visibility. A packet may be visible but fail to lie in a same-scale canonical core if the surrounding zero configuration is sparse, clustered, or if symmetry partners fall outside the scale window.

The failure modes are:

\[
\text{packet enters same-scale window boundary},
\]

\[
\text{required symmetry partner leaves same-scale window},
\]

\[
\text{scale ratio hits an endpoint},
\]

\[
\text{selected packet core changes cardinality under symmetry closure}.
\]

These are controlled semianalytic walls, but unlike \(q^{(7)}=0\), it is not automatic that every off-line packet has a nearby admissible \(m\) inside the same-scale region.

So here the adversary finds a genuine missing theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Same-scale non-swallowing is not proved by the local packet calculation.}
}
\]

Needed statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Every RH-relevant nondegenerate off-line packet admits a nearby sampling midpoint for which the symmetry packet is same-scale eligible.}
}
\]

Without this, the reduced-base architecture may miss packets that are always outside the selected same-scale core.

This is not a contradiction; it is a global reach dependency.

---

## 5. Gram nondegeneracy is probably proper incidence, but not fully proved

The relevant Gram obstruction is

\[
\det G_{\core}=0
\]

or fixed-sector Gram degeneration. Locally, determinants are analytic functions of the packet/core parameters. If one can exhibit a single good point with

\[
\det G_{\core}\ne0,
\]

then the degeneracy locus is proper on that connected stratum.

For the local two-jet phase kernel, the same-point Gram block has the model form

\[
G_0
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
q_0 & 0\\
0 & \frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''
\end{pmatrix}
\]

at a centred even packet. Good-patch positivity is

\[
q_0>0,\qquad \frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''>0.
\]

A packet contribution alone can make \(q''\) large and negative, so pure packet data may fail positivity. But the full zeta phase includes background terms, and for large height \(q_0\) is typically dominated by the positive background slope. Thus there is no local packet identity forcing Gram degeneracy.

Still, a rigorous proof needs an actual good-patch nondegeneracy theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\det G_{\core}\not\equiv0
\text{ on each RH-relevant reduced packet stratum.}
}
\]

So Gram degeneracy is likely a proper incidence wall, but its non-swallowing is a dependency.

---

## 6. \(K_1\)-visibility collapse is proper incidence

The \(K_1\)-visibility wall is

\[
\Xi_{K_1}=0
\]

or, in the centred one-whitening-slot route,

\[
yBr=0.
\]

We have:

\[
y_{\rm lead}=-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72},
\]

\[
B\propto q'',
\]

\[
r=\frac12(g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2}).
\]

For a nondegenerate off-line packet on a good patch,

\[
A_2\ne0,\qquad q''=2A_2\ne0,
\]

and \(r=0\) is just the Gram-isotropy wall \(g=h\). Hence \(K_1\)-visibility collapse is not structurally forced.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\Xi_{K_1}=0
\text{ is a proper analytic incidence wall, unless a new hidden }K_1\text{ identity exists.}
}
\]

No such identity is currently visible.

---

## 7. Can all walls jointly cover the admissible family?

This is the adversarial core.

A finite union of proper analytic walls cannot cover a connected open set in the local parameter space. Thus if the off-line packet has a genuine local parameter neighbourhood in \((m,s,\theta)\), and each wall is proper, then there exists a point outside all walls.

The only ways the walls can jointly swallow the packet are:

1. the admissible family is lower-dimensional and lies inside one wall by definition;
2. same-scale eligibility is never satisfied;
3. core-gap ties are identically forced by a distinct competing packet;
4. Gram nondegeneracy fails identically;
5. the local construction fixes \(m=\gamma/2\), collapsing midpoint freedom;
6. the \(K_1\)-transport coefficient vanishes by an unrecognized identity.

We have good reasons to reject 1, 5, and 6 in the current draft architecture. Cases 2, 3, and 4 remain genuine reach dependencies.

Thus the strongest theorem currently supportable is conditional:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If same-scale eligibility, packet-level core-gap, and Gram nondegeneracy are not identically failing, then every nondegenerate off-line packet reaches }\mathcal B_{\rm red}^{+}.
}
\]

---

## 8. Reduced-base reach theorem, adversarial form

\[
\boxed{
\begin{minipage}{0.9\linewidth}
Let \(\mathcal P(\rho)\) be a nondegenerate off-line symmetry packet. Suppose
there is a local admissible sampling neighbourhood in which:
\[
\text{(i) }m\text{ is a free sampling midpoint,}
\]
\[
\text{(ii) the packet is same-scale eligible somewhere,}
\]
\[
\text{(iii) the packet-level core-gap wall is not identically forced,}
\]
\[
\text{(iv) the core and fixed-sector Gram determinants are not identically zero,}
\]
and
\[
\text{(v) the }K_1\text{ visibility coefficient is not identically zero.}
\]
Then the packet meets
\[
\mathcal B_{\rm red}^{+}.
\]
Equivalently, the excluded walls do not swallow the packet family.
\end{minipage}
}
\]

This is the best reduced-base reach statement I can defend.

---

## 9. Kill-condition assessment

The rank-two route loses universal reach if any of the following can occur for an actual off-line packet:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-scale ineligibility for all admissible }m,
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{identically tied competing packet scores at the selected core boundary,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\det G_{\core}\equiv0
\text{ or fixed-sector Gram degeneracy on the packet stratum,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\Xi_{K_1}\equiv0
\text{ despite }q^{(7)}\not\equiv0.
}
\]

Of these, the first three are the serious adversarial targets. The fourth looks unlikely after the one-whitening-slot and \(q^{(7)}\) analyses.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{Reduced-base reach survives adversarial testing, but remains conditional.}
}
\]

I do **not** find a structural reason actual off-line packets must be trapped inside

\[
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\core}\cup\mathcal E_{\rm invis}^{\core,7}.
\]

The visibility walls are proper. Symmetry does not swallow packets if one orders symmetry packets rather than individual zeros. Midpoint freedom defeats the centred-parity obstruction.

The remaining dependencies are global reach conditions:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-scale eligibility, packet-level core-gap nonidentity, and Gram nondegeneracy.}
}
\]

So Agent 2’s conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal B_{\rm red}^{+}\text{ reach is plausible and locally supported, but not yet a theorem for every RH-relevant off-line packet.}
}
\]

The next mathematical attack should focus on proving same-scale/core-gap/Gram non-swallowing for actual zeta packets, not on more local visibility algebra.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 20:59:25)

_Thought for 10s_

# Agent 2 — Same-scale / Core-gap / Gram Non-swallowing Attack

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Same-scale/core-gap/Gram non-swallowing is plausible but not proved from the current local formalism.}
}
\]

I can reduce each failure mode to an explicit analytic or semianalytic incidence condition. I do **not** find a structural reason that a nondegenerate off-line packet must be swallowed by these walls. However, I also cannot prove the global reach theorem for every RH-relevant packet without a new zeta-specific input about the surrounding zero configuration.

So the best defensible statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If an off-line packet has one admissible same-scale neighbourhood with strict packet gap and nondegenerate Gram block, then it reaches }\mathcal B_{\rm red}^{+}.
}
\]

The missing theorem is exactly the non-swallowing implication:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal P(\rho)\ \text{off-line nondegenerate}
\Longrightarrow
\exists m,s
\text{ with same-scale eligibility, strict packet core gap, and }\det G_{\core}\ne0.
}
\]

This matches the strategic update: local rank-two infrastructure is frozen, and the remaining live local-ish dependency is reach/non-swallowing for the reduced base. 

---

## 1. Same-scale eligibility

Let \(P=\mathcal P(\rho)\) be an off-line symmetry packet and let \(D_P(m)\) be its packet distance score. A typical same-scale rule has the form

\[
c_1S(m)\le D_P(m)\le c_2S(m),
\]

or, for a packet core \(\mathcal C_k(m)\),

\[
\frac{\max_{P_i\in\mathcal C_k(m)}D_{P_i}(m)}
{\min_{P_i\in\mathcal C_k(m)}D_{P_i}(m)}
\le C_{\rm scale}.
\]

The failure set is a finite union of equality walls such as

\[
D_P(m)=c_1S(m),
\qquad
D_P(m)=c_2S(m),
\]

and scale-ratio boundary equations. On a fixed labelled packet stratum these are semianalytic.

So same-scale failure is **controlled**, but control is not reach. The adversarial possibility is:

\[
P\text{ is never same-scale eligible for any admissible }m.
\]

This cannot be ruled out by local Taylor algebra alone because it depends on the surrounding zero configuration and the chosen scale window.

A sufficient theorem would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{For every nondegenerate RH-relevant packet }P,\text{ there exists }m
\text{ with }P\text{ in the same-scale admissible window.}
}
\]

Without that, \(\mathcal B_{\rm red}^{+}\) may miss some off-line packets before visibility or transport even begins.

---

## 2. Packet-level core-gap non-swallowing

Use packet ordering, not individual zero ordering. Let

\[
D_{P_1}(m)\le D_{P_2}(m)\le\cdots
\]

be the ordered packet scores. The strict core-gap condition is

\[
\Delta_k^{\rm pkt}(m)
=
D_{P_{k+1}}(m)-D_{P_k}(m)>0.
\]

The failure wall is

\[
D_{P_{k+1}}(m)=D_{P_k}(m).
\]

Again, on a fixed labelled stratum this is semianalytic.

The only way this wall swallows a full local admissible interval is if two distinct packet-score functions are identically tied:

\[
D_P(m)\equiv D_Q(m)
\]

for a competing packet \(Q\ne P\), or if \(k\) cuts through a symmetry shell. The second issue is fixed by packet-level ordering and symmetry-compatible \(k\). The first is a genuine degeneracy condition on the zero configuration.

Thus the right local theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
D_P-D_Q\not\equiv0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\{D_P=D_Q\}\text{ is a proper wall.}
}
\]

The missing zeta-specific theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Distinct RH-relevant packets do not have identically tied packet scores on the whole admissible sampling family, except on controlled symmetry/collision strata.}
}
\]

So core-gap non-swallowing is conditionally positive but not closed.

---

## 3. Gram nondegeneracy

At the local two-jet level, the centred same-point Gram model has the schematic form

\[
G_0
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
q_0 & 0\\
0 & \frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Good-patch nondegeneracy requires

\[
q_0\ne0,
\qquad
\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''\ne0
\]

and, for positivity,

\[
q_0>0,
\qquad
\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''>0.
\]

For a pure centred packet, \(q''\) can be large and negative, so positivity is not automatic from packet data alone. But the actual zeta phase has background slope, and the local formalism usually works on a good patch where the Gram block is already assumed nondegenerate.

The danger is not local vanishing at isolated points. It is the identity

\[
\det G_{\core}(m)\equiv0
\]

on the entire admissible packet family.

That would be a severe degeneracy of the local kernel model. I see no packet-symmetry reason for it. But proving

\[
\boxed{
\det G_{\core}\not\equiv0
}
\]

for every RH-relevant reduced packet stratum requires either an explicit Gram determinant formula or a general positivity theorem for the phase-kernel Gram block in the chosen good-patch regime.

So Gram non-swallowing is also a dependency:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Gram degeneracy is a controlled analytic wall, but non-identity must be proved per reduced stratum.}
}
\]

---

## 4. Combined non-swallowing theorem

The natural theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\begin{minipage}{0.9\linewidth}
Let \(\mathcal P(\rho)\) be a nondegenerate off-line symmetry packet. Suppose
there is an admissible local sampling neighbourhood \(U\) such that:
\[
\text{(i) }\mathcal P(\rho)\text{ is same-scale eligible at some }m\in U,
\]
\[
\text{(ii) no distinct competing packet has packet score identically tied with it on }U,
\]
\[
\text{(iii) the core and fixed-sector Gram determinants are not identically zero on }U,
\]
and
\[
\text{(iv) the visibility channel is not identically zero.}
\]
Then there exists \(b=(m,s,\mathcal C_k(m))\in\mathcal B_{\rm red}^{+}\) for
that packet.
\end{minipage}
}
\]

The proof is standard analytic avoidance: a finite union of proper analytic or semianalytic walls cannot cover a connected open admissible neighbourhood.

---

## 5. Adversarial failure modes

The rank-two route still fails universal reach if any of the following can happen for an actual off-line zero packet:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-scale ineligibility for every admissible midpoint;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{identically tied distinct packet scores at the selected core boundary;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\det G_{\core}\equiv0
\text{ or }\det G_{\mathfrak f}\equiv0
\text{ on the admissible packet stratum;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\Xi_{K_1}\equiv0
\text{ despite }q^{(7)}\not\equiv0.
}
\]

The last looks unlikely after the order-seven visibility analysis. The first three are genuine reach dependencies and require zeta-specific or core-rule-specific proof.

---

## 6. Final status

\[
\boxed{
\text{Agent 2 cannot break reduced-base reach, but cannot close it unconditionally.}
}
\]

The local analytic structure is favourable: the dangerous walls are proper unless new identities are imposed. But the global theorem

\[
\boxed{
\text{every RH-relevant off-line packet meets }\mathcal B_{\rm red}^{+}
}
\]

remains open.

The next mathematical attack should focus on proving these three non-swallowing facts for actual zeta packets:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-scale eligibility somewhere,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict packet-level core gap somewhere,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Gram nondegeneracy somewhere.}
}
\]

Without those, the rank-two transported finite-core program has local coherence but not universal reach.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 21:02:58)

I’ll run Agent 2 as the pilot incidence-function agent. I’ll test whether a concrete wall such as \(q^{(7)}(m)=0\) or \(g-h=0\) admits a polynomial separation mechanism, rather than merely qualitative avoidance.

_Thought for 24s_

# Agent 2 — Pilot Incidence-Function Attack

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The pilot wall }q^{(7)}=0\text{ is locally avoidable, but not yet quantitatively separated for actual total zeta data.}
}
\]

For the **isolated off-line packet contribution**, one can get a polynomial lower bound by choosing a non-centered midpoint with controlled offset. But for the **actual reduced-core derivative**

\[
q_{\core}^{(7)}(m),
\]

other core/background packets can in principle cancel the packet contribution. The current framework does not yet prevent super-polynomial approach to

\[
q_{\core}^{(7)}(m)=0.
\]

So the pilot result is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{qualitative local avoidance: yes;}
\qquad
\text{actual zeta polynomial incidence separation: still missing.}
}
\]

This matches the current strategic state: the local rank-two framework is coherent, but the missing gate is a zeta-specific quantitative separation theorem. 

---

## 1. Pilot incidence function

Use the lowest explicit visibility wall:

\[
\Phi_7(m):=q_{\core}^{(7)}(m).
\]

For an isolated off-line packet

\[
\rho=\beta+i\gamma,
\qquad
\delta=\beta-\frac12,
\qquad
a=\frac12-\delta,
\qquad
b=\frac12+\delta,
\]

with

\[
x_m=2m-\gamma,
\]

the paired packet contribution is

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)
=
\frac{a}{a^2+x^2}
+
\frac{b}{b^2+x^2},
\qquad
x=2t-\gamma.
\]

Its seventh derivative has the exact form

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m)
=
-5160960x_m
\left[
\frac{
a(x_m^2-a^2)(x_m^4-6a^2x_m^2+a^4)
}{
(a^2+x_m^2)^8
}
+
\frac{
b(x_m^2-b^2)(x_m^4-6b^2x_m^2+b^4)
}{
(b^2+x_m^2)^8
}
\right].
\]

Near the centered point \(x_m=0\),

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m)
=
5160960(a^{-9}+b^{-9})x_m+O(x_m^3).
\]

Since \(a,b>0\),

\[
5160960(a^{-9}+b^{-9})>0.
\]

Thus \(x_m=0\) is a simple zero of the isolated packet’s seventh derivative.

---

## 2. Polynomial separation for the isolated packet

Assume \(0<a,b<1\), and choose

\[
|x_m|\le c_0\min(a,b)
\]

with \(c_0\) small enough. Then the cubic remainder is controlled, so

\[
\left|
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m)
\right|
\ge
C(a,b)|x_m|,
\]

where one may take

\[
C(a,b)\asymp a^{-9}+b^{-9}.
\]

In particular, if the sampling architecture permits a non-centered offset

\[
|x_m|=Q^{-A},
\]

and this offset stays inside the same reduced-good-patch component, then

\[
\boxed{
\left|
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m)
\right|
\gg
(a^{-9}+b^{-9})Q^{-A}.
}
\]

So for the **isolated packet wall**

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}=0,
\]

polynomial avoidance is easy: do not choose \(m\) too close to the packet center.

This confirms that the wall

\[
q^{(7)}=0
\]

is not locally dangerous because of packet symmetry alone.

---

## 3. Why this does not yet solve actual zeta incidence separation

The actual incidence function is not just the isolated packet derivative. It is

\[
q_{\core}^{(7)}(m)
=
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m)
+
R_{\core}^{(7)}(m),
\]

where \(R_{\core}^{(7)}\) contains the rest of the selected core and background/local phase contribution.

Even if

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m)\gg Q^{-A},
\]

the total derivative can still be small if

\[
R_{\core}^{(7)}(m)
\approx
-f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m).
\]

The current local theory has no theorem excluding

\[
\left|
q_{\core}^{(7)}(m)
\right|
<
Q^{-B}
\]

or even

\[
\left|
q_{\core}^{(7)}(m)
\right|
<
e^{-Q}
\]

along actual zeta samples.

So the isolated-packet calculation proves properness of the wall, but not quantitative separation of the actual zeta trajectory from that wall.

---

## 4. Remez/Łojasiewicz reduction

Suppose \(\Phi_7(m)=q_{\core}^{(7)}(m)\) is analytic on a compact reduced-good interval \(I\). A Łojasiewicz inequality gives

\[
|\Phi_7(m)|
\ge
c\,\operatorname{dist}(m,Z_{\Phi_7})^\mu,
\]

where

\[
Z_{\Phi_7}=\{m:\Phi_7(m)=0\}.
\]

This only helps if we also know

\[
\operatorname{dist}(m,Z_{\Phi_7})\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

Without that, the actual sampled midpoint \(m=m(Q)\) could satisfy

\[
0<\operatorname{dist}(m(Q),Z_{\Phi_7})<e^{-Q},
\]

and then \(\Phi_7(m(Q))\) is nonzero but too small for the needed finite-core lower bound.

Thus the pilot wall confirms the central obstruction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{analytic nonvanishing is insufficient; we need polynomial distance from incidence.}
}
\]

---

## 5. Second pilot: Gram-isotropy wall \(g-h=0\)

The centred one-whitening-slot route uses

\[
r=\frac12(g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2}),
\]

so the wall is

\[
g=h.
\]

At the local two-jet level,

\[
g=\frac{q_0}{\pi},
\qquad
h=\frac1\pi\left(\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''\right).
\]

Thus

\[
g-h=0
\]

is equivalent to

\[
q''=6q_0-2q_0^3.
\]

For the clean isolated packet with

\[
\delta=\frac14,
\qquad
q_0=\frac{16}{3},
\qquad
q''=-\frac{14336}{27},
\]

whereas

\[
6q_0-2q_0^3=-\frac{7328}{27}.
\]

So

\[
g-h\ne0.
\]

Again, this proves the wall is not a structural identity. It does **not** prove that actual zeta data cannot approach

\[
g-h=0
\]

super-polynomially closely.

The same Remez/Łojasiewicz issue appears.

---

## 6. Third pilot: quotient determinant \(\omega(A_7,A_5)\)

A more RH-relevant scalar incidence function is

\[
\Phi_{\det}(m)
=
\omega_h(A_7^{\mathfrak f}(m),A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)).
\]

For the non-centered \(q^{(7)}\) channel, the \(K_1\)-slice is \(I\)-directional, so

\[
A_{7,K_1}^{\mathfrak f}
=
u_7 I
\]

with \(u_7\ne0\). Hence

\[
\Phi_{\det}^{(K_1)}
=
u_7v_5.
\]

If

\[
v_5\ne0,
\]

then the determinant detects the \(K_1\)-visibility channel.

But \(v_5\) itself can vanish on an incidence wall. Also, the full determinant includes other \(A_7\)-components. So again, the determinant is not identically zero outside incidence, but a quantitative lower bound requires

\[
\operatorname{dist}
\bigl(
\theta(m),
\{\omega(A_7,A_5)=0\}
\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

No such theorem is currently available.

---

## 7. What a successful pilot theorem would need

For any chosen scalar incidence function \(\Phi\), the needed theorem has the form:

\[
\boxed{
|\Phi(\theta(m))|
\ge
Q^{-A}
}
\]

outside controlled incidence.

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
\theta(m),
\{\Phi=0\}
\right)
\ge
Q^{-A'}.
}
\]

For \(\Phi=q_{\core}^{(7)}\), this would require an actual zeta theorem preventing cancellation between the distinguished packet derivative and the rest of the reduced core:

\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}^{(7)}(m)+R_{\core}^{(7)}(m)
\]

from being too small unless it is genuinely on the incidence wall.

For \(\Phi=g-h\), it would require polynomial avoidance of the Gram-isotropy hypersurface.

For \(\Phi=\omega(A_7,A_5)\), it would require polynomial avoidance of the determinant-alignment locus.

These are all versions of the same missing theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual zeta data must stay polynomially far from transported analytic incidence.}
}
\]

---

## 8. Adversarial model

The failure mode is simple.

Let \(\Phi\) be any analytic incidence function with \(\Phi\not\equiv0\). A formal sampled path can satisfy

\[
\Phi(\theta(Q))=e^{-Q}
\]

while never entering \(\{\Phi=0\}\).

All qualitative local arguments still hold:

\[
\Phi\ne0,
\]

visibility is active,

the bundle exists,

and the local affine forcing equation holds.

But any desired finite-core lower bound of the form

\[
|\Phi|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

fails.

Therefore no pilot incidence function closes the proof without a genuinely quantitative input.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{Pilot incidence functions confirm the problem; they do not solve it.}
}
\]

For the isolated packet,

\[
q^{(7)}(m)
\]

has a simple zero at \(x_m=0\), and a controlled non-centered choice gives a polynomially large packet contribution. But for actual reduced-core zeta data, other core/background contributions may cancel it to super-polynomial accuracy. The current theory does not exclude that.

The same pattern holds for

\[
g-h,
\qquad
\omega_h(A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\]

and likely for any low-order scalar incidence function.

Thus Agent 2’s conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The next theorem cannot be qualitative incidence avoidance. It must be quantitative zeta-specific separation.}
}
\]

A viable theorem would be:

\[
\boxed{
\max_j|\Phi_j(\theta(m))|\ge Q^{-A_j}
}
\]

for a finite family of transported incidence functions \(\Phi_j\), or directly

\[
\boxed{
\|P_{\rm fd}^{\rm tr}(\theta(m))+J_{\rm fd}^{\rm tr}(\theta(m))\|_\infty
\ge Q^{-B}.
}
\]

Without such a theorem, the rank-two framework remains locally coherent but globally non-closing.

_Thought for 17s_

# Agent 2 — Pair / Interference Decomposition for a Pilot Alignment Coordinate

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{\rm align}
=
D_7
=
\omega_h(A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f})
\text{ decomposes cleanly into pair and interaction pieces, but the decomposition does not prevent cancellation.}
}
\]

The clean decomposition is:

\[
\Phi_{\rm align}
=
\Phi_P+\Phi_J
\]

with

\[
\Phi_P
=
\omega_h(A_{7,P}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\qquad
\Phi_J
=
\omega_h(A_{7,J}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}).
\]

The obstruction remains:

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_J\approx-\Phi_P
}
\]

is still algebraically possible. Thus this coordinate exposes the same global gate identified in the handover: we need a zeta-specific quantitative anti-cancellation theorem, not merely another local decomposition. 

---

## 1. Coordinate under test

Take the first quotient/transverse transported alignment coordinate:

\[
\Phi_{\rm align}
=
D_7
=
\omega_h(A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
\]

where

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}=u_7I+v_7S,
\qquad
A_5^{\mathfrak f}=u_5I+v_5S,
\]

and

\[
\omega_h(A,B)=u_Av_B-v_Au_B.
\]

Thus

\[
\boxed{
D_7=u_7v_5-u_5v_7.
}
\]

This is the cleanest scalar alignment coordinate because it detects whether \(A_7^{\mathfrak f}\) lies along the reference line \(\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}\). It is also exactly the coordinate where the earlier quotient architecture encountered the aligned branch.

---

## 2. Pair / interaction split

Write

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
=
A_{7,P}^{\mathfrak f}
+
A_{7,J}^{\mathfrak f},
\]

where:

\[
A_{7,P}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\text{single-packet / pair-like contribution},
\]

and

\[
A_{7,J}^{\mathfrak f}
=
\text{interaction contribution from mixed packets, whitening interactions, and core coupling}.
\]

Then

\[
D_7
=
\omega_h(A_{7,P}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f})
+
\omega_h(A_{7,J}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}).
\]

Define

\[
\boxed{
D_{7,P}
:=
\omega_h(A_{7,P}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}),
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
D_{7,J}
:=
\omega_h(A_{7,J}^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}).
}
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
D_7=D_{7,P}+D_{7,J}.
}
\]

So the pair/interference structure is exactly the same as the previously identified finite-core alignment problem:

\[
\boxed{
D_{7,J}\approx -D_{7,P}.
}
\]

---

## 3. Expanding the reference \(A_5\)

If the fifth-order reference itself is decomposed,

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}
=
A_{5,P}^{\mathfrak f}
+
A_{5,J}^{\mathfrak f},
\]

then

\[
D_7
=
\omega_h(A_{7,P},A_{5,P})
+
\omega_h(A_{7,P},A_{5,J})
+
\omega_h(A_{7,J},A_{5,P})
+
\omega_h(A_{7,J},A_{5,J}).
\]

Thus the fully expanded decomposition is

\[
\boxed{
D_7
=
D_{PP}+D_{PJ}+D_{JP}+D_{JJ}.
}
\]

The most useful split is:

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_P:=D_{PP}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_J:=D_{PJ}+D_{JP}+D_{JJ}.
}
\]

If \(A_5\) is treated as a fixed reference section rather than decomposed dynamically, then the earlier simpler split

\[
D_7=D_{7,P}+D_{7,J}
\]

is preferable.

Either way, interaction terms remain available to cancel pair terms.

---

## 4. Distinguished packet contribution

Let \(P_\rho=\mathcal P(\rho)\) be the distinguished off-line packet. Its local \(K_1\) visibility contribution has the form

\[
A_{7,\rho}^{\mathfrak f}
=
c_\rho V_\rho,
\]

where \(V_\rho\in\mathfrak f_h\) is the fixed-sector direction and \(c_\rho\) is the scalar packet coefficient.

For the non-centered \(q^{(7)}\)-route,

\[
c_\rho
\sim
q_\rho^{(7)}(m),
\]

and near the packet center,

\[
q_\rho^{(7)}(m)
=
5160960(a^{-9}+b^{-9})x_m+O(x_m^3).
\]

For the centered one-whitening-slot route,

\[
c_\rho
\sim
-2yBr,
\]

with

\[
y_{\rm lead}=-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72},
\qquad
B\propto q'',
\qquad
r=\frac12(g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2}).
\]

Thus the distinguished packet contributes

\[
D_{7,\rho}
=
\omega_h(c_\rho V_\rho,A_5)
=
c_\rho\,\omega_h(V_\rho,A_5).
\]

So

\[
\boxed{
D_{7,\rho}\ne0
}
\]

outside the visibility incidence walls

\[
c_\rho=0
\quad\text{or}\quad
\omega_h(V_\rho,A_5)=0.
\]

But this only proves the pair contribution is nonzero. It does not prevent cancellation by the rest of the core.

---

## 5. Rest-of-core and interaction contribution

Write

\[
A_{7,P}^{\mathfrak f}
=
A_{7,\rho}^{\mathfrak f}
+
A_{7,\mathrm{rest}}^{P,\mathfrak f}.
\]

Then

\[
D_7
=
\omega_h(A_{7,\rho},A_5)
+
\omega_h(A_{7,\mathrm{rest}}^P,A_5)
+
\omega_h(A_{7,J},A_5).
\]

Define

\[
D_{7,\rho}:=\omega_h(A_{7,\rho},A_5),
\]

and

\[
D_{7,\mathrm{int}}
:=
\omega_h(A_{7,\mathrm{rest}}^P,A_5)
+
\omega_h(A_{7,J},A_5).
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
D_7
=
D_{7,\rho}
+
D_{7,\mathrm{int}}.
}
\]

The bad branch is now explicit:

\[
\boxed{
D_{7,\mathrm{int}}
\approx
-D_{7,\rho}.
}
\]

The current local theory gives

\[
D_{7,\rho}\ne0
\]

outside incidence, but it gives no lower bound on

\[
D_{7,\rho}+D_{7,\mathrm{int}}.
\]

---

## 6. Why this coordinate does not avoid cancellation

The determinant coordinate is linear in \(A_7\):

\[
D_7=\omega_h(A_7,A_5).
\]

Therefore if the interaction part can realize a component in the same determinant direction as the packet part, then cancellation is possible.

Formally, choose

\[
A_{7,\rho}=V,
\]

and choose interaction data so that

\[
\omega_h(A_{7,J},A_5)
=
-\omega_h(V,A_5)+\varepsilon.
\]

Then

\[
D_7=\varepsilon.
\]

This can be arbitrarily small while

\[
D_{7,\rho}\ne0,
\qquad
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\ne0
\]

unless additional geometry prevents \(A_{7,J}\) from occupying the required direction.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
D_7\text{ is a good diagnostic but not a protected invariant.}
}
\]

It detects alignment, but does not forbid it.

---

## 7. Transported derivative coordinate

A natural strengthened coordinate is

\[
\Psi_7
:=
\omega_h(\nabla_\kappa^{\mathfrak f}A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_5^{\mathfrak f}).
\]

Using the affine forcing equation,

\[
\nabla_\kappa^{\mathfrak f}A_7^{\mathfrak f}
=
E_{\eta\eta}+E_{\rm lower},
\]

we get

\[
\Psi_7
=
\omega_h(E_{\eta\eta},A_5)
+
\omega_h(E_{\rm lower},A_5).
\]

The active row contributes

\[
\omega_h(E_{\eta\eta},A_5)
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}
\omega_h(wI+uS,A_5).
\]

This is a strong local forcing diagnostic. But again the lower terms may cancel:

\[
\omega_h(E_{\rm lower},A_5)
\approx
-\omega_h(E_{\eta\eta},A_5).
\]

So \(\Psi_7\) has the same structural issue unless the \(E_{\rm lower}\) table gives a sign, a cone, a line-orthogonality condition, or a quantitative lower bound.

At present it does not.

---

## 8. Combined coordinate

A stronger transported coordinate vector is

\[
\Phi_{\rm tr}
=
\left(
\omega_h(A_7,A_5),
\,
\omega_h(\nabla_\kappa^{\mathfrak f}A_7,A_5)
\right).
\]

Then

\[
\Phi_{\rm tr}
=
\Phi_{\rm tr,P}
+
\Phi_{\rm tr,J}.
\]

The aligned branch becomes

\[
\Phi_{\rm tr,J}\approx-\Phi_{\rm tr,P}.
\]

This is harder to arrange than scalar cancellation, but still possible in formal local models unless pair and interaction components are constrained to transverse cones or quantitatively separated incidence strata.

Thus even the two-coordinate transported vector does not close the proof by itself.

---

## 9. What theorem this decomposition demands

For the scalar coordinate \(D_7\), the needed theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\left|
D_{7,P}(\theta(m))+D_{7,J}(\theta(m))
\right|
\ge
Q^{-B}
}
\]

outside controlled incidence.

For the transported vector coordinate, the needed theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\left\|
\Phi_{\rm tr,P}(\theta(m))
+
\Phi_{\rm tr,J}(\theta(m))
\right\|_\infty
\ge
Q^{-B}.
}
\]

Equivalently, one needs a distance bound

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
\theta(m),
\mathcal Z_{\Phi_{\rm tr}}
\cup
\mathcal E_{\rm geom}^{\rm tr}
\right)
\ge
Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The decomposition itself does not provide this. It only identifies the anti-cancellation theorem required.

---

## 10. Agent 2 conclusion

\[
\boxed{
\text{The pair/interference decomposition exposes, but does not solve, the main obstruction.}
}
\]

For the pilot alignment coordinate

\[
D_7=\omega_h(A_7,A_5),
\]

we have

\[
\boxed{
D_7=D_{7,\rho}+D_{7,\mathrm{int}},
}
\]

where \(D_{7,\rho}\) is the distinguished off-line packet contribution and \(D_{7,\mathrm{int}}\) contains rest-of-core and interaction effects.

The local theory can certify

\[
D_{7,\rho}\ne0
\]

outside visibility incidence. It cannot currently prevent

\[
D_{7,\mathrm{int}}\approx -D_{7,\rho}.
\]

The same conclusion holds for the transported derivative coordinate

\[
\omega_h(\nabla_\kappa^{\mathfrak f}A_7,A_5).
\]

Thus the next theorem must be quantitative and zeta-specific:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual zeta data cannot make the interaction contribution cancel the packet contribution to super-polynomial accuracy outside incidence.}
}
\]

Without that, the rank-two transported finite-core framework remains a precise local language for the obstruction, not a proof of RH.
## User (2026-04-25 21:10:18)

Do the agent instruction in the message as per your agent number.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 21:10:29)

_Thought for 9s_

# Agent 2 — Pair/rest decomposition for \(\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}\)

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}=-2yBr
\text{ is explicit and locally nonzero, but it is still vulnerable to rest/core cancellation through }q'',\,y,\text{ and }g-h.
}
\]

The distinguished packet term is not protected by sign, cone, or determinant structure. Once \(q_0\), \(q''\), \(A_2\), \(g\), and \(h\) are treated as **full-core** quantities rather than isolated-packet quantities, the rest of the core can oppose the packet contribution in multiple independent ways. This is exactly the obstruction flagged in the attached instruction: the agents must now lock to \(\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}\), not drift to \(D_7\) or \(\Phi_{\rm lock}\). 

---

## 1. The scalar and its useful form

The coordinate is

\[
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}
=
-2yBr,
\]

where

\[
B=
\frac{q''}{4\pi\sqrt{gh}(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)},
\]

and

\[
r=\frac12(g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2}).
\]

Thus

\[
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}
=
-y\,
\frac{q''(g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2})}
{4\pi\sqrt{gh}(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)}.
\]

Using

\[
g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2}
=
\frac{h-g}{\sqrt{gh}(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)},
\]

this becomes

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}
=
-y\,
\frac{q''(h-g)}
{4\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

This form makes the weak factors explicit:

\[
y,\qquad q'',\qquad h-g.
\]

---

## 2. Full-core variables

Write

\[
q_0=q_{0,P}+q_{0,R},
\]

\[
q''=q''_P+q''_R,
\]

\[
A_2=A_{2,P}+A_{2,R}.
\]

At the centered distinguished packet,

\[
q''_P=2A_{2,P},
\]

and

\[
A_{2,P}
=
-4(a^{-3}+b^{-3})\ne0.
\]

But the scalar \(\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}\) uses the **full** \(q_0,q'',A_2,g,h\), not only packet components. In particular,

\[
g=\frac{q_0}{\pi},
\]

and

\[
h=\frac1\pi\left(\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''\right).
\]

Therefore

\[
h-g
=
\frac1\pi
\left(
\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''-q_0
\right).
\]

The rest of the core enters not only additively in \(A_2\) and \(q''\), but also nonlinearly through \(q_0^3\) and the denominator.

---

## 3. Leading \(y\)-expansion

Use the leading \(K_1\) mixed-block coefficient

\[
y_{\rm lead}
=
-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72}.
\]

Then

\[
\Phi_{K_1,\lead}^{(7,I)}
=
\frac{q_0A_2^2q''(g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2})}
{288\pi\sqrt{gh}(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)}.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{K_1,\lead}^{(7,I)}
=
\frac{q_0A_2^2q''(h-g)}
{288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

Substituting

\[
A_2=A_{2,P}+A_{2,R},
\qquad
q''=q''_P+q''_R,
\]

the numerator before Gram denominators is

\[
q_0A_2^2q''
=
(q_{0,P}+q_{0,R})(A_{2,P}+A_{2,R})^2(q''_P+q''_R).
\]

Expand:

\[
(A_{2,P}+A_{2,R})^2
=
A_{2,P}^2+2A_{2,P}A_{2,R}+A_{2,R}^2.
\]

Hence

\[
\begin{aligned}
q_0A_2^2q''
={}&
(q_{0,P}+q_{0,R})
(A_{2,P}^2+2A_{2,P}A_{2,R}+A_{2,R}^2)
(q''_P+q''_R)\\
={}&
q_{0,P}A_{2,P}^2q''_P\\
&+
q_{0,R}A_{2,P}^2q''_P\\
&+
2q_0A_{2,P}A_{2,R}q''_P\\
&+
q_0A_{2,P}^2q''_R\\
&+
q_0A_{2,R}^2q''_P\\
&+
2q_0A_{2,P}A_{2,R}q''_R\\
&+
q_0A_{2,R}^2q''_R,
\end{aligned}
\]

where the terms containing \(q_0\) in the last five lines use the full

\[
q_0=q_{0,P}+q_{0,R}.
\]

Using \(q''_P=2A_{2,P}\), the pure distinguished-packet leading numerator is

\[
\boxed{
q_{0,P}A_{2,P}^2q''_P
=
2q_{0,P}A_{2,P}^3.
}
\]

The leading full-core scalar is therefore schematically

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{K_1,\lead}^{(7,I)}
=
\frac{
\bigl[
2q_{0,P}A_{2,P}^3
+
\text{rest/mixed terms}
\bigr](h-g)
}
{288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

---

## 4. Pair/rest decomposition

Define the distinguished-packet contribution by retaining only the packet-only numerator but using the full Gram denominator and full anisotropy factor:

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_P
:=
\frac{
q_{0,P}A_{2,P}^2q''_P(h-g)
}
{288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_P
=
\frac{
2q_{0,P}A_{2,P}^3(h-g)
}
{288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

This is the cleanest definition if the goal is to isolate the distinguished packet’s leading \(K_1\) contribution while preserving the fact that whitening uses full-core Gram data.

Then define

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{\rm rest}
:=
\Phi_{K_1,\lead}^{(7,I)}-\Phi_P.
}
\]

Decompose

\[
\Phi_{\rm rest}
=
\Phi_{q_0\text{-rest}}
+
\Phi_{A_2\text{-mix}}
+
\Phi_{q''\text{-rest}}
+
\Phi_{\rm Gram/aniso}
+
\Phi_{\rm denom}
+
\Phi_{\rm higher}.
\]

The first three numerator-level pieces are:

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{q_0\text{-rest}}
=
\frac{
q_{0,R}A_{2,P}^2q''_P(h-g)
}
{288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{A_2\text{-mix}}
=
\frac{
2q_0A_{2,P}A_{2,R}q''_P(h-g)
+
q_0A_{2,R}^2q''_P(h-g)
}
{288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{q''\text{-rest}}
=
\frac{
q_0A_{2,P}^2q''_R(h-g)
+
2q_0A_{2,P}A_{2,R}q''_R(h-g)
+
q_0A_{2,R}^2q''_R(h-g)
}
{288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}.
}
\]

The Gram/aniso term is more subtle because

\[
h-g
=
\frac1\pi
\left(
\frac13(q_{0,P}+q_{0,R})^3
+
\frac16(q''_P+q''_R)
-
(q_{0,P}+q_{0,R})
\right).
\]

So even if the numerator \(q_0A_2^2q''\) has a nonzero distinguished-packet part, the rest can oppose it through

\[
h-g.
\]

---

## 5. Is \(\Phi_P\) sign-definite?

Only under extra assumptions.

At a centered packet,

\[
A_{2,P}<0,
\]

so

\[
A_{2,P}^3<0.
\]

If

\[
q_{0,P}>0,
\]

then

\[
2q_{0,P}A_{2,P}^3<0.
\]

The denominator

\[
288\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2
\]

is positive on a positive Gram patch.

Therefore the sign of \(\Phi_P\) is controlled by

\[
\operatorname{sgn}((A_{2,P}^3)(h-g)).
\]

Since \(A_{2,P}^3<0\),

\[
\operatorname{sgn}(\Phi_P)
=
-\operatorname{sgn}(h-g)
\]

assuming \(q_{0,P}>0\).

But \(h-g\) is a **full-core** quantity. It is not packet-only. It can change sign when background/rest terms vary. Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_P\text{ is not intrinsically sign-definite unless }h-g\text{ is sign-controlled.}
}
\]

This is the first major vulnerability.

---

## 6. Which rest terms can oppose \(\Phi_P\)?

Several independent classes can oppose it.

### 6.1 \(q_0\)-rest opposition

The term

\[
q_{0,R}A_{2,P}^2q''_P
\]

has the same \(A_{2,P}^2q''_P\) dependence as the packet contribution. If \(q_{0,R}\) has opposite phase/sign relative to \(q_{0,P}\), it can oppose. Even if \(q_{0,R}>0\), it can change the magnitude and sign indirectly through \(h-g\).

### 6.2 \(A_2\)-mixing opposition

The term

\[
2q_0A_{2,P}A_{2,R}q''_P
\]

can oppose the packet term if

\[
A_{2,R}
\]

has the appropriate sign/phase. Since \(A_{2,R}\) is a sum of other core packet curvatures and background curvature contributions, no local formal rule prevents this.

### 6.3 \(q''\)-rest opposition

The term

\[
q_0A_{2,P}^2q''_R
\]

is especially dangerous because it directly modifies the \(q''\) factor. If

\[
q''_R\approx -q''_P,
\]

then

\[
q''=q''_P+q''_R
\]

can be made small without crossing \(q''=0\) exactly. This can make

\[
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}
\]

super-polynomially small.

### 6.4 Gram anisotropy opposition

Since

\[
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}
\propto h-g,
\]

any rest/background tuning that makes

\[
h-g
\]

small suppresses the scalar regardless of the packet numerator. This is probably the weakest point.

The wall

\[
g=h
\]

is explicitly excluded, but exclusion is qualitative. It does not give

\[
|g-h|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

### 6.5 Denominator effects

The denominator is positive on a positive Gram patch, but it can be large:

\[
gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2.
\]

If \(g,h\) grow with \(Q\), the denominator contributes negative powers of \(Q\). This is acceptable if polynomially controlled, but it still must be tracked.

---

## 7. Does the formula force \(\Phi_{\rm rest}\) to be lower order?

No.

The rest terms are the **same formal order** as the packet term.

For example:

\[
q_{0,R}A_{2,P}^2q''_P
\]

has exactly the same \(A_{2,P}^2q''_P\) factor as the packet term, replacing only \(q_{0,P}\) by \(q_{0,R}\).

Similarly,

\[
q_0A_{2,P}^2q''_R
\]

has the same source-jet degree if

\[
q''_R
\]

is same-scale with

\[
q''_P.
\]

Therefore there is no formal hierarchy:

\[
|\Phi_{\rm rest}|\ll|\Phi_P|
\]

from local rank-two algebra alone.

A lower-order claim would require a **same-scale arithmetic** or **core-selection** theorem showing rest packets are smaller, farther, or differently scaled. That is not currently available.

---

## 8. Can obstruction be reduced to \(g-h\)?

Not fully.

The factorization

\[
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}
=
-y\,
\frac{q''(h-g)}
{4\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2}
\]

shows three independent dangerous factors:

\[
y,\qquad q'',\qquad h-g.
\]

At leading order,

\[
y=-\frac{q_0A_2^2}{72}.
\]

So \(y\) is vulnerable through

\[
q_0=0
\quad\text{or}\quad
A_2=0.
\]

On a good patch \(q_0\neq0\), but \(A_2=A_{2,P}+A_{2,R}\) can be small if rest curvature cancels packet curvature.

The \(q''\) factor is essentially the same vulnerability:

\[
q''=2A_2.
\]

Thus \(y\) and \(q''\) are both controlled by the same full-core curvature cancellation issue.

The Gram anisotropy \(h-g\) is an additional independent weak point:

\[
h-g
=
\frac1\pi\left(\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''-q_0\right).
\]

So the obstruction does not reduce to \(g-h\) alone. It reduces to two full-core cancellation conditions:

\[
\boxed{
A_2\text{ not too small}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
g-h\text{ not too small}.
}
\]

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
|A_2|\ge Q^{-A_1},
\qquad
|g-h|\ge Q^{-A_2}.
}
\]

---

## 9. Classification

\[
\boxed{
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}\text{ is vulnerable.}
}
\]

It is locally explicit and nonzero for the isolated packet, but once full-core quantities are used, there are several same-order cancellation channels:

\[
q''_R\approx -q''_P,
\]

\[
A_{2,R}\approx -A_{2,P},
\]

\[
h-g\approx0,
\]

and mixed \(q_0\)-rest effects.

No local algebra prevents these.

The exact quantitative theorem needed is:

\[
\boxed{
|A_2|\ge Q^{-A_1},
\qquad
|g-h|\ge Q^{-A_2},
\qquad
q_0,g,h\text{ polynomially bounded above/below}.
}
\]

Then, since at leading order

\[
|\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}|
\asymp
\frac{|q_0|\,|A_2|^2\,|q''|\,|g-h|}
{|g h|(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2},
\]

and \(q''=2A_2\), one would get a polynomial lower bound.

But those assumptions are not proved.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{The pair/rest decomposition shows the chosen scalar is not protected.}
}
\]

For

\[
\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}
=
-y\,
\frac{q''(h-g)}
{4\pi gh(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)^2},
\]

the distinguished packet contributes a nonzero leading term, but the rest of the core can oppose it through:

\[
q''_R,
\qquad
A_{2,R},
\qquad
q_{0,R},
\qquad
h-g.
\]

There is no formal lower-order suppression of the rest term. The coordinate is therefore **vulnerable**, not protected.

The next theorem must be quantitative:

\[
\boxed{
\text{actual zeta data must satisfy polynomial lower bounds for the full-core curvature }A_2
\text{ and Gram anisotropy }g-h,
}
\]

or more directly,

\[
\boxed{
|\Phi_{K_1}^{(7,I)}(\theta(m))|\ge Q^{-A}.
}
\]

Without that, \(-2yBr\) is an excellent diagnostic of the obstruction, but it does not close the proof.

Do the agent instruction in the message as per your agent number.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 21:14:49)

_Thought for 13s_

# Agent 2 — Log-derivative ODE / Phase-jet Attack

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No local ODE, monotonicity law, or phase-jet identity currently prevents }g\approx h.
}
\]

Equivalently, I find no structural reason that actual zeta data must satisfy

\[
\left|q''-6q+2q^3\right|\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

The one-packet model is **not** near Gram isotropy at its center, and for large positive background \(q_0\), the term \(2q_0^3\) tends to push

\[
q''-6q_0+2q_0^3
\]

large. But sums of packets plus background terms can tune \(q\) and \(q''\) independently enough that near-isotropy

\[
q''=6q-2q^3+\varepsilon
\]

is formally compatible with \(q>0\), \(g>0\), \(h>0\), same-scale packets, and the current rank-two local axioms. The attached prompt asks exactly whether this phase/log-derivative relation can be ruled out; my answer is no, not from the present local machinery. 

---

## 1. The target functional

The Gram anisotropy is

\[
g-h
=
\frac1\pi
\left(
q-\frac13q^3-\frac16q''
\right).
\]

Thus

\[
g=h
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
q''=6q-2q^3.
\]

Define

\[
\Gamma_G[q]
:=
q''-6q+2q^3.
\]

Then

\[
g-h=-\frac{\Gamma_G[q]}{6\pi}.
\]

The desired theorem would be a quantitative separation estimate

\[
|\Gamma_G[q](m)|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

on the reduced good patch, outside the explicit Gram-isotropy incidence wall.

---

## 2. One-packet computation at the center

For one centred off-line packet,

\[
f(t)
=
\frac{a}{a^2+x^2}
+
\frac{b}{b^2+x^2},
\qquad
x=2t-\gamma,
\]

with

\[
a=\frac12-\delta,\qquad b=\frac12+\delta,
\qquad 0<a,b<1.
\]

At \(x=0\),

\[
f_0=f(m)=a^{-1}+b^{-1},
\]

and

\[
f''(m)
=
-8(a^{-3}+b^{-3}).
\]

Therefore

\[
\Gamma_G[f](m)
=
-8(a^{-3}+b^{-3})
-
6(a^{-1}+b^{-1})
+
2(a^{-1}+b^{-1})^3.
\]

Using

\[
(a^{-1}+b^{-1})^3
=
a^{-3}+b^{-3}
+
3a^{-2}b^{-1}
+
3a^{-1}b^{-2},
\]

we get

\[
\Gamma_G[f](m)
=
-6(a^{-3}+b^{-3})
+
6(a^{-2}b^{-1}+a^{-1}b^{-2})
-
6(a^{-1}+b^{-1}).
\]

Equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\Gamma_G[f](m)
=
6\left[
a^{-1}b^{-1}(a^{-1}+b^{-1})
-
(a^{-3}+b^{-3})
-
(a^{-1}+b^{-1})
\right].
}
\]

For the clean packet

\[
a=\frac14,\qquad b=\frac34,
\]

we have

\[
f_0=\frac{16}{3},
\qquad
f''=-\frac{14336}{27}.
\]

Then

\[
6f_0-2f_0^3
=
-\frac{7328}{27},
\]

so

\[
\Gamma_G[f](m)
=
f''-6f_0+2f_0^3
=
-\frac{7008}{27}
=
-\frac{2336}{9}
\ne0.
\]

Thus a single centred packet is not structurally Gram-isotropic. This is useful, but it is only a non-identity witness.

---

## 3. One-packet behaviour near the center

Near \(x=0\), the packet is even:

\[
f(x)
=
f_0+c_2x^2+c_4x^4+\cdots,
\]

so

\[
f'(0)=0.
\]

The functional

\[
\Gamma_G[f]
=
f''-6f+2f^3
\]

is also even near the center. Therefore, if

\[
\Gamma_G[f](0)\ne0,
\]

then it remains nonzero in a small centred neighbourhood. For the clean packet, the centered value is already bounded away from zero in local packet units.

But this again only shows that the packet contribution alone is not stuck on \(g=h\). It does not control the full core sum.

---

## 4. Sums of packets: no additive preservation

The functional

\[
\Gamma_G[q]=q''-6q+2q^3
\]

is nonlinear. For a sum

\[
q=f+r,
\]

we have

\[
\Gamma_G[f+r]
=
\Gamma_G[f]
+
\Gamma_G[r]
+
6f^2r
+
6fr^2.
\]

Indeed,

\[
2(f+r)^3
=
2f^3+6f^2r+6fr^2+2r^3.
\]

Thus Gram anisotropy is **not additive** over packets. Rest/core contributions can modify \(\Gamma_G\) at the same formal order as the distinguished packet.

This is the central obstruction: even if

\[
\Gamma_G[f]\ne0
\]

for the distinguished packet, another same-scale contribution \(r\) can push

\[
\Gamma_G[f+r]
\]

toward zero.

---

## 5. Tuning with a positive background

Let the rest/background contribution be approximately constant near \(m\):

\[
r(t)=c,
\qquad c>0.
\]

Then

\[
r''=0,
\]

and

\[
q=f+c.
\]

At the packet center,

\[
\Gamma_G[f+c]
=
f''
-
6(f+c)
+
2(f+c)^3.
\]

As a function of \(c\),

\[
\Gamma_G[f+c]
=
2c^3+6fc^2+(6f^2-6)c+(f''-6f+2f^3).
\]

This cubic in \(c\) has a lot of flexibility. Its derivative is

\[
6c^2+12fc+6f^2-6
=
6\bigl((c+f)^2-1\bigr).
\]

For large \(c\), it is positive and

\[
\Gamma_G[f+c]\sim 2c^3.
\]

So a large positive background tends to make \(\Gamma_G\) large and positive. This supports the heuristic that if \(q_0\sim \log T\) is large and \(q''\) is not comparably negative, then

\[
|\Gamma_G|\asymp q_0^3.
\]

But this is not a theorem, because \(q''\) from the rest/core can be negative and of comparable size.

---

## 6. Direct tuning of \(q''\)

The Gram-isotropy condition is

\[
q''=6q-2q^3.
\]

On a positive Gram patch,

\[
g=\frac q\pi>0,
\qquad
h=\frac1\pi\left(\frac13q^3+\frac16q''\right)>0.
\]

At the wall, substituting \(q''=6q-2q^3\),

\[
h
=
\frac1\pi\left(
\frac13q^3+\frac16(6q-2q^3)
\right)
=
\frac q\pi
=
g.
\]

So the wall is compatible with

\[
g>0,\qquad h>0
\]

for every \(q>0\). There is no positivity contradiction.

Moreover, if we take

\[
q''=6q-2q^3+\varepsilon,
\]

then

\[
\Gamma_G=\varepsilon,
\]

and

\[
h-g=\frac{\varepsilon}{6\pi}.
\]

For arbitrarily small nonzero \(\varepsilon\), we remain in a positive Gram patch as long as \(q>0\). Thus Gram positivity alone does not prevent super-polynomial nearness to the wall.

---

## 7. Can packet sums realize this tuning?

At the formal phase-jet level, yes.

Each packet contributes to \(q\) and \(q''\) by

\[
f_0=a^{-1}+b^{-1},
\qquad
f''=-8(a^{-3}+b^{-3})
\]

at its center, and off-center variants give additional flexibility. Background/gamma-like terms can raise \(q\) substantially. Additional same-scale packets can contribute negative curvature \(q''\) with adjustable magnitude.

Thus one can tune the pair \((q,q'')\) toward

\[
(q,\;6q-2q^3)
\]

without forcing \(q=0\), \(q''=0\), or Gram degeneracy. The current local axioms do not impose an arithmetic discreteness or separation condition preventing such tuning to accuracy \(e^{-Q}\).

This does **not** prove actual zeta zeros can tune this way. It proves the present local phase-jet model cannot rule it out.

---

## 8. Riccati / log-derivative structure

One might hope \(q\) satisfies a Riccati-type equation inherited from a log derivative. If

\[
q
\]

were the imaginary or real part of a logarithmic derivative of a second-order ODE solution, a differential relation might exist.

But the zeta \(\xi\)-function does not presently supply a closed finite-order ODE of the form

\[
q''=F(q,q')+\text{controlled error}.
\]

The Hadamard product gives

\[
q
=
\text{background}
+
\sum_\rho \text{packet kernel},
\]

so \(q''\) is another independent sum of differentiated kernels. There is no local algebraic relation forcing

\[
q''-6q+2q^3
\]

to have sign or size. The cubic term \(2q^3\) is a nonlinear expression of the sum \(q\), while \(q''\) is a linear sum of second derivatives. Their near-cancellation is exactly the kind of nonlinear incidence the current framework cannot exclude.

So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{No Riccati-type or finite-order phase ODE is presently available to prevent }g\approx h.
}
\]

---

## 9. Actual zeta heuristic

If in the intended height regime

\[
q_0\asymp \log T
\]

and if

\[
q''=O(\log^C T)
\]

with \(C<3\), then

\[
\Gamma_G
=
q''-6q_0+2q_0^3
\asymp
2q_0^3
\]

would be large. This would solve the Gram-anisotropy problem.

But this heuristic needs an actual bound on \(q''\) in the reduced good patch. Near zeros or tightly clustered packets, \(q''\) can be large. Same-scale off-line packets may contribute curvature of comparable size to powers of \(q_0\). The current draft does not establish

\[
|q''|\ll q_0^3-Q^{-A}
\]

or any one-sided variant.

Thus the promising path is not a local identity; it is a global analytic estimate:

\[
\boxed{
\text{show }q''\text{ cannot track }6q_0-2q_0^3\text{ to super-polynomial accuracy.}
}
\]

---

## 10. Final classification

For the target estimate

\[
|\Gamma_G|\ge Q^{-A},
\qquad
\Gamma_G=q''-6q+2q^3,
\]

my classification is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{plausible in some height regimes, but currently missing; false in flexible zeta-like phase-jet models.}
}
\]

The flexible model is:

\[
q>0,\qquad
q''=6q-2q^3+\varepsilon,
\qquad
0<|\varepsilon|<e^{-Q}.
\]

Then

\[
g,h>0,
\qquad
g\ne h,
\]

but

\[
0<|g-h|=\frac{|\varepsilon|}{6\pi}<e^{-Q}.
\]

Nothing in the current local rank-two framework excludes this.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{There is no current log-derivative ODE or phase-jet law preventing near Gram-isotropy.}
}
\]

The one-packet model is non-isotropic, and large positive background may make Gram anisotropy large. But packet sums and background terms can formally tune

\[
q''-6q+2q^3
\]

arbitrarily close to zero while preserving

\[
q>0,\qquad g>0,\qquad h>0.
\]

Therefore the estimate

\[
\boxed{
|q''-6q+2q^3|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

must be treated as a new zeta-specific quantitative theorem, not a consequence of the present local phase geometry.
## User (2026-04-25 21:20:26)

## Assistant (2026-04-25 21:20:30)

I’ll specialize the whitening algebra at \(g=h\). The key question is whether higher-order whitening terms can convert the skew \(J\)-carrier back into the fixed \(I/S\) sector after the order-7 \(I\)-component dies.

_Thought for 51s_

# Agent 2 — Gram-Isotropy Specialization Attack

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{At }g=h,\text{ fixed-sector leakage is possible at the next whitening order.}
}
\]

The order-7 fixed-sector channel dies at Gram isotropy because

\[
X_0=g^{-1/2}I
\]

is scalar, so the one-whitening-slot commutator

\[
X_1YX_0-X_0YX_1
\]

lands in the anti-fixed \(D\)-sector. But at the next whitening level, the mixed term

\[
\boxed{
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1
}
\]

has a fixed \(I\)-component whenever the second whitening coefficient \(X_2\) has a \(D\)-component. Generically it does. Thus \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) has a plausible intra-rank-two escape route on the wall \(g=h\).

This does **not** close the proof. It moves the weak factor from first Gram anisotropy

\[
g-h
\]

to second Gram anisotropy, essentially

\[
\boxed{
(1-q^2)q''-\frac{q^{(4)}}6.
}
\]

So the next question becomes whether this second anisotropy is nonzero and quantitatively separated on actual zeta data. The attached prompt asks precisely whether \(A_9^{\mathfrak f}\) can survive on \(g=h\), rather than re-proving that \(|g-h|\) is missing. 

---

## 1. Specializing the leading whitening at \(g=h\)

At the Gram-isotropy wall,

\[
g=h,
\]

the leading Gram block is scalar:

\[
G_0=gI,
\qquad
X_0=G_0^{-1/2}=g^{-1/2}I.
\]

At centred parity,

\[
G_+(s)=G_-(-s),
\qquad
X_+(s)=X_-(-s),
\]

so

\[
X_{+,j}=(-1)^jX_{-,j}.
\]

Write

\[
X_j:=X_{-,j}.
\]

The first whitening coefficient is

\[
X_1=BS,
\]

with

\[
B=\frac{q''}{8\pi g^{3/2}}
\]

on the wall \(g=h\), equivalently the specialization of

\[
B=
\frac{q''}{4\pi\sqrt{gh}(\sqrt g+\sqrt h)}.
\]

Let the skew quadratic mixed block be

\[
Y=yJ.
\]

Then the order-7 one-slot channel is

\[
T_7^{(1)}
=
X_1YX_0+X_0YX_{+,1}
=
X_1YX_0-X_0YX_1.
\]

Since \(X_0\) is scalar,

\[
T_7^{(1)}
=
g^{-1/2}y(X_1J-JX_1).
\]

Using

\[
X_1=BS,
\]

and

\[
SJ=-D,\qquad JS=D,
\]

we get

\[
X_1J-JX_1
=
B(SJ-JS)
=
B(-D-D)
=
-2BD.
\]

Thus

\[
\boxed{
T_7^{(1)}=-2yBg^{-1/2}D.
}
\]

Therefore

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_7^{(1)}=0
}
\]

at \(g=h\). The order-7 signal survives only in the anti-fixed \(D\)-sector.

---

## 2. Structure of \(X_2\) at \(g=h\)

Write the same-point Gram expansion as

\[
G_-(s)
=
G_0+sH_1+s^2H_2+O(s^3).
\]

At centred parity,

\[
H_1\in \mathbf C S,
\]

and

\[
H_2\in \mathbf C I\oplus \mathbf C D.
\]

Because \(G_0=gI\) is scalar, the binomial expansion gives

\[
G_-(s)^{-1/2}
=
g^{-1/2}
\left(
I+g^{-1}(sH_1+s^2H_2+\cdots)
\right)^{-1/2}.
\]

Hence

\[
X_1
=
-\frac12g^{-3/2}H_1,
\]

and

\[
X_2
=
-\frac12g^{-3/2}H_2
+
\frac38g^{-5/2}H_1^2.
\]

Since

\[
H_1\in\mathbf C S
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
H_1^2\in\mathbf C I,
\]

the \(D\)-component of \(X_2\) comes entirely from the \(D\)-component of \(H_2\).

Thus write

\[
\boxed{
X_2=A I+C D.
}
\]

The coefficient \(C\) is generically nonzero unless the second Gram variation is isotropic.

---

## 3. Computing the \(D\)-part of \(X_2\)

Use the local same-point Gram model

\[
G(t)
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
q(t) & \frac12q'(t)\\[1mm]
\frac12q'(t) & \frac13q(t)^3+\frac16q''(t)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

At a centred even point,

\[
q'(m)=q'''(m)=0.
\]

Then

\[
G_0
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
q_0 & 0\\
0 & \frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

The wall \(g=h\) is exactly

\[
q_0=\frac13q_0^3+\frac16q''.
\]

Also

\[
G_-(s)=G(m-s/2),
\]

so

\[
H_2=\frac18G''(m).
\]

Compute

\[
G''(m)
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
q'' & 0\\
0 & q_0^2q''+\frac16q^{(4)}
\end{pmatrix},
\]

where the \(2q(q')^2\) term vanishes at the centred even point.

Therefore

\[
(H_2)_D
=
\frac{1}{16\pi}
\left(
q''-q_0^2q''-\frac16q^{(4)}
\right)D.
\]

Thus

\[
C
=
-\frac12g^{-3/2}\cdot
\frac{1}{16\pi}
\left(
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}
\right).
\]

So

\[
\boxed{
C
=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}g^{-3/2}
\left(
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}
\right).
}
\]

At \(g=h\), substitute

\[
q''=6q_0-2q_0^3
\]

if desired, but that does not force \(C=0\). It would force \(C=0\) only if the further condition

\[
\boxed{
q^{(4)}=6(1-q_0^2)q''
}
\]

also holds.

That is a new second-isotropy wall, not the original Gram-isotropy wall.

---

## 4. The first fixed-sector leakage at \(g=h\)

Now examine the order-9-type cross term

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
X_1YX_{+,2}+X_2YX_{+,1}.
\]

At centred parity,

\[
X_{+,2}=X_2,
\qquad
X_{+,1}=-X_1.
\]

Therefore

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1.
\]

Substitute

\[
X_1=BS,
\qquad
X_2=AI+CD,
\qquad
Y=yJ.
\]

Then

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
yB\left(SJ(AI+CD)-(AI+CD)JS\right).
\]

Use

\[
SJ=-D,
\qquad
JS=D.
\]

Then

\[
SJ(AI+CD)
=
-D(AI+CD)
=
-AD-CI,
\]

and

\[
(AI+CD)JS
=
(AI+CD)D
=
AD+CI.
\]

Therefore

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
yB\left((-AD-CI)-(AD+CI)\right)
=
-2yB(AD+CI).
\]

So

\[
\boxed{
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yBA\,D
-
2yBC\,I.
}
\]

Projecting to the fixed sector,

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yBC\,I.
}
\]

Thus, at \(g=h\),

\[
\boxed{
\text{the first candidate order-9 fixed-sector survivor is }-2yBC\,I.
}
\]

It is nonzero if

\[
\boxed{
y\ne0,\qquad B\ne0,\qquad C\ne0.
}
\]

The old \(g-h\) factor is gone. It is replaced by the second anisotropy coefficient \(C\).

---

## 5. Other nearby terms

### 5.1 \(X_1YX_1\)

At \(g=h\),

\[
X_1YX_1
=
B^2ySJS.
\]

Since

\[
SJS=-J,
\]

this is skew:

\[
X_1YX_1=-B^2yJ.
\]

Hence

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_1YX_1)=0.
}
\]

So two first-whitening slots do not yield fixed-sector leakage.

### 5.2 \(X_2YX_0+X_0YX_2\)

Since \(X_0\) is scalar,

\[
X_2YX_0+X_0YX_2
=
g^{-1/2}y(X_2J+JX_2).
\]

With

\[
X_2=AI+CD,
\]

\[
X_2J+JX_2
=
2AJ+C(DJ+JD).
\]

But

\[
DJ=S,\qquad JD=-S,
\]

so

\[
DJ+JD=0.
\]

Thus

\[
X_2J+JX_2=2AJ,
\]

which is skew and killed by \(\pi_{\mathfrak f}\).

Therefore

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_2YX_0+X_0YX_2)=0.
}
\]

### 5.3 \(X_3YX_0-X_0YX_3\)

At centred parity \(X_3\) is expected to lie in the \(S\)-sector. If

\[
X_3=C_3S,
\]

then

\[
X_3J-JX_3
=
C_3(SJ-JS)
=
-2C_3D,
\]

again anti-fixed.

Thus

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_3YX_0-X_0YX_3)=0
}
\]

unless \(X_3\) has a \(D\)-component, which is not expected under centred parity.

---

## 6. Interpretation

At \(g=h\), fixed-sector leakage is not possible from the same one-slot mechanism because \(X_0\) is scalar. But it becomes possible once the skew carrier interacts with an **odd whitening coefficient** \(X_1\) and an **even anisotropic whitening coefficient** \(X_2\).

The mechanism is:

\[
S\cdot J\cdot D - D\cdot J\cdot S
\quad\leadsto\quad
I.
\]

Concretely,

\[
\boxed{
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1
\text{ converts the skew }J\text{ carrier into a fixed }I\text{ component.}
}
\]

This is the order-9 analogue of the order-7 conversion, but it uses second rather than first Gram anisotropy.

---

## 7. What replaces the weak factor \(g-h\)?

The order-7 scalar contained

\[
r=\frac12(g^{-1/2}-h^{-1/2}),
\]

so it died at \(g=h\).

The order-9 candidate contains

\[
C=(X_2)_D,
\]

with

\[
C
=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}g^{-3/2}
\left(
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}
\right).
\]

Thus the new weak factor is

\[
\boxed{
\Gamma_{G,2}
:=
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}.
}
\]

At \(g=h\), this becomes

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}
=
(1-q_0^2)(6q_0-2q_0^3)-\frac16q^{(4)}.
\]

The order-9 route avoids needing

\[
|g-h|\ge Q^{-A},
\]

but it now needs

\[
\boxed{
|\Gamma_{G,2}|\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

or a proof that \(\Gamma_{G,2}\not\equiv0\) and cannot approach zero too closely for actual zeta data.

---

## 8. Final classification

\[
\boxed{
\text{At }g=h,\text{ fixed-sector leakage is possible at second whitening order.}
}
\]

The first viable mechanism is

\[
\boxed{
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1,
}
\]

with

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yBC\,I.
}
\]

Here

\[
B=\frac{q''}{8\pi g^{3/2}},
\]

and

\[
C=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}g^{-3/2}
\left(
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}
\right).
\]

Therefore the order-9 fixed-sector survivor is nonzero if

\[
\boxed{
y\ne0,\qquad q''\ne0,\qquad
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\ne0.
}
\]

This is a genuine rank-two fixed-sector escape from first Gram isotropy, but it does **not** eliminate the need for quantitative incidence separation. It shifts the next weak wall from

\[
g-h=0
\]

to the second anisotropy wall

\[
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}=0.
\]

Thus Agent 2’s conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
A_9^{\mathfrak f}|_{g=h}
\text{ has a plausible nonzero fixed-sector channel, namely }-2yBC\,I.
}
\]

The next computation should verify the order bookkeeping and check whether other order-9 summands cancel this channel.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 21:25:15)

_Thought for 14s_

# Agent 2 — Exact \((X_2)_D\) Coefficient

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
(X_2)_D
=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}\,g^{-3/2}
\left((1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\right).
}
\]

With the centred parity convention

\[
X_1=BS,\qquad Y=yJ,\qquad X_2=AI+CD,
\]

the order-9 candidate satisfies

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}
\left(
X_{-,1}YX_{+,2}+X_{-,2}YX_{+,1}
\right)
=
\frac{y\,q''}{128\pi^2 g^3}
\left((1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\right)I.
}
\]

Thus the order-9 fixed-sector survivor is controlled by the second Gram-anisotropy factor

\[
\boxed{
\Gamma_{G,2}:=(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}.
}
\]

This is the exact coefficient, modulo the convention that \(G_2\) denotes the literal \(s^2\)-coefficient in \(G_-(s)=G(m-s/2)\). The attached prompt identifies this \((X_2)_D\) computation as the key order-9 micro-target. 

---

## 1. Expansion convention

Use

\[
G_-(s)=G_0+sG_1+s^2G_2+O(s^3),
\]

with

\[
G_-(s)=G(m-s/2).
\]

Therefore

\[
G_1=-\frac12G'(m),
\qquad
G_2=\frac18G''(m).
\]

At \(g=h\),

\[
G_0=gI.
\]

Let

\[
X_-(s)=G_-(s)^{-1/2}
=
X_0+sX_1+s^2X_2+O(s^3).
\]

For a scalar base \(G_0=gI\), the binomial expansion gives

\[
X_0=g^{-1/2}I,
\]

\[
X_1=-\frac12g^{-3/2}G_1,
\]

and

\[
X_2
=
-\frac12g^{-3/2}G_2
+
\frac38g^{-5/2}G_1^2.
\]

---

## 2. The \(D\)-part of \(X_2\)

At a centred even point,

\[
q'(m)=q'''(m)=0.
\]

The local same-point Gram model is

\[
G(t)
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
q(t) & \frac12q'(t)\\
\frac12q'(t) & \frac13q(t)^3+\frac16q''(t)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Thus

\[
G''(m)
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
q'' & 0\\
0 & q_0^2q''+\frac16q^{(4)}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Since

\[
G_2=\frac18G''(m),
\]

we get

\[
G_2
=
\frac1{8\pi}
\begin{pmatrix}
q'' & 0\\
0 & q_0^2q''+\frac16q^{(4)}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

For a diagonal matrix \(\operatorname{diag}(a,d)\),

\[
\operatorname{diag}(a,d)
=
\frac{a+d}{2}I+\frac{a-d}{2}D.
\]

Therefore

\[
(G_2)_D
=
\frac{1}{16\pi}
\left(
q''-q_0^2q''-\frac16q^{(4)}
\right).
\]

So

\[
\boxed{
(G_2)_D
=
\frac{1}{16\pi}
\left((1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\right).
}
\]

Now \(G_1\in\mathbf C S\), hence

\[
G_1^2\in\mathbf C I.
\]

Thus \(G_1^2\) contributes no \(D\)-component to \(X_2\). Therefore

\[
(X_2)_D
=
-\frac12g^{-3/2}(G_2)_D,
\]

and hence

\[
\boxed{
(X_2)_D
=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}g^{-3/2}
\left((1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\right).
}
\]

---

## 3. Exact order-9 channel coefficient

At centred parity,

\[
X_{+,1}=-X_{-,1},\qquad X_{+,2}=X_{-,2}.
\]

Write

\[
X_1:=X_{-,1}=BS,
\]

\[
X_2:=X_{-,2}=AI+CD,
\]

where

\[
C=(X_2)_D.
\]

Let

\[
Y=yJ.
\]

Then

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
X_{-,1}YX_{+,2}+X_{-,2}YX_{+,1}
=
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1.
\]

Using

\[
SJ=-D,\qquad JS=D,
\]

we compute

\[
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1
=
yB\left(SJ(AI+CD)-(AI+CD)JS\right).
\]

Now

\[
SJ(AI+CD)=-(AD+CI),
\]

and

\[
(AI+CD)JS=(AI+CD)D=AD+CI.
\]

Thus

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yB(AD+CI).
\]

The fixed-sector projection keeps the \(I\)-part and kills \(D\), so

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yBC\,I.
}
\]

Substituting

\[
C
=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}g^{-3/2}\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

where

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}
=
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)},
\]

gives

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
\frac{yB}{16\pi}g^{-3/2}\Gamma_{G,2}\,I.
}
\]

At \(g=h\), the first whitening coefficient is

\[
B
=
\frac{q''}{8\pi g^{3/2}}.
\]

Therefore

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
\frac{y\,q''}{128\pi^2g^3}
\left((1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\right)I.
}
\]

---

## 4. Nonvanishing conditions

The order-9 fixed-sector channel is nonzero if

\[
\boxed{
y\ne0,
\qquad
q''\ne0,
\qquad
\Gamma_{G,2}\ne0,
\qquad
g>0.
}
\]

The old order-7 weak factor

\[
g-h
\]

does not appear. On the Gram-isotropy wall \(g=h\), the order-9 channel instead depends on

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}
=
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}.
\]

So the order-9 mechanism genuinely escapes first Gram isotropy, but introduces a second anisotropy wall:

\[
\boxed{
\Gamma_{G,2}=0.
}
\]

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{Exact coefficient computed.}
}
\]

With the literal \(s^2\)-coefficient convention

\[
G_2=\frac18G''(m),
\]

we have

\[
\boxed{
(X_2)_D
=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}g^{-3/2}
\left((1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\right).
}
\]

Consequently,

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}
\left(
X_{-,1}YX_{+,2}+X_{-,2}YX_{+,1}
\right)\bigg|_{g=h}
=
\frac{y\,q''}{128\pi^2g^3}
\left((1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}\right)I.
}
\]

Thus the order-9 fixed-sector survivor is real, conditional on degree bookkeeping and on no forced cancellation by other order-9 terms. The next weak wall is

\[
\boxed{
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}=0.
}
\]

# Agent 2 — \(K_1\)-Channel Independence Audit

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}
\text{ is an independent order-9 fixed-sector channel, modulo a same-channel analytic identity.}
}
\]

The candidate row

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
X_{-,1}YX_{+,2}+X_{-,2}YX_{+,1}
\]

has a distinctive channel label:

\[
\boxed{
\text{skew }K_1\text{ carrier }Y_J
\quad+\quad
\text{odd whitening slot }X_{1,S}
\quad+\quad
\text{even anisotropic whitening slot }X_{2,D}.
}
\]

At \(g=h\), it produces

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yB(X_2)_D I.
}
\]

Using the exact coefficient

\[
(X_2)_D
=
-\frac{1}{32\pi}g^{-3/2}\Gamma_{G,2},
\qquad
\Gamma_{G,2}:=(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)},
\]

this is

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
\frac{yq''}{128\pi^2g^3}\Gamma_{G,2}\,I.
}
\]

So the channel is nonzero under

\[
\boxed{
y\ne0,\qquad q''\ne0,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}\ne0,\qquad g>0.
}
\]

The attached prompt identifies this exact term as the order-9 fixed-sector survivor to audit. 

---

## 1. Formal channel labels

The row has three essential labels:

\[
Y=yJ,
\]

\[
X_1=BS,
\]

\[
X_2=A I+C D,
\qquad C=(X_2)_D.
\]

Thus the live part is

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}.
\]

More explicitly, at centered parity,

\[
X_{+,1}=-X_{-,1},
\qquad
X_{+,2}=X_{-,2},
\]

so

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1.
\]

Substitute:

\[
X_1=BS,\qquad Y=yJ,\qquad X_2=A I+C D.
\]

Then

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
yB\left(SJ(AI+CD)-(AI+CD)JS\right).
\]

Using

\[
SJ=-D,\qquad JS=D,
\]

we get

\[
SJ(AI+CD)=-(AD+CI),
\]

and

\[
(AI+CD)JS=(AI+CD)D=AD+CI.
\]

Therefore

\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yB(AD+CI).
\]

The fixed-sector projection kills \(D\) and keeps \(I\), hence

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yBC\,I.
}
\]

This is a clean \(I\)-sector row.

---

## 2. Comparison with the order-7 channel

At order 7, the live centered one-whitening-slot row was

\[
T_7^{(1)}
=
X_1YX_0-X_0YX_1.
\]

With

\[
X_0=pI+rD,
\]

the fixed-sector part was

\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_7^{(1)}
=
-2yBr\,I.
\]

Thus the order-7 channel had labels

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{0,D}.
\]

It died at \(g=h\) because

\[
X_{0,D}=rD=0.
\]

The order-9 channel replaces first anisotropy

\[
X_{0,D}
\]

with second anisotropy

\[
X_{2,D}.
\]

So the channel hierarchy is:

\[
\boxed{
A_7^{\mathfrak f}:\quad Y_JX_{1,S}X_{0,D}
}
\]

and

\[
\boxed{
A_9^{\mathfrak f}:\quad Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}.
}
\]

This is a genuine new channel, not the old order-7 channel in disguise.

---

## 3. Could another order-9 row have the same labels?

Within the convolution structure

\[
X_-(s)N(s)X_+(s),
\]

the only terms with one \(X_1\), one \(X_2\), and the same \(Y=yJ\) carrier are exactly the two endpoint-parity companions:

\[
X_{-,1}YX_{+,2},
\qquad
X_{-,2}YX_{+,1}.
\]

Their centered-parity sum is precisely

\[
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1.
\]

So the candidate already includes the full same-label endpoint-symmetrized pair.

There is no third distinct row with the same formal label

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}
\]

unless the draft’s \(N\)-expansion contains another independent skew \(J\)-carrier at the same internal degree with exactly the same source scalar \(y\). If so, that is not cross-channel cancellation; it is a redefinition of the total \(Y\)-carrier:

\[
y\mapsto y_{\rm total}.
\]

Then the incidence wall becomes

\[
y_{\rm total}=0.
\]

---

## 4. Other possible order-9 rows are different-channel rows

### 4.1 Direct \(N_9\)

A direct row

\[
X_0N_9X_0
\]

has no \(X_1\) and no \(X_2\). It cannot share the label

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}.
\]

It may contribute to the \(I\)-sector, but it is a different raw-numerator channel. To cancel the candidate identically, it would need an analytic identity tying its raw \(N_9\) scalar to

\[
yB(X_2)_D.
\]

That is not forced by sector algebra.

### 4.2 \(X_1N_7X_1\)

This has two \(X_1\)-slots and no \(X_2\)-slot. If \(N_7\) is skew \(J\), then

\[
X_1JX_1
=
B^2SJS
=
-B^2J,
\]

which is skew and killed by fixed-sector projection. If \(N_7\) has other sectors, the channel labels differ.

So it cannot cancel \(Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}\) structurally.

### 4.3 \(X_2N_7X_0+X_0N_7X_2\)

This has \(X_2\) but no \(X_1\). It lacks the odd whitening slot \(B\). It is not the same channel.

If \(N_7=J\), then with \(X_0\) scalar at \(g=h\),

\[
X_2JX_0+X_0JX_2
\]

is skew or \(S\)-cancelling depending on the \(X_2\)-sector; in the basic \(X_2=A I+C D\) case, the \(D\)-part cancels in the anticommutator and the \(A\)-part remains \(J\), hence no \(I\)-projection.

### 4.4 \(X_3YX_0-X_0YX_3\)

At centered parity, \(X_3\) is expected to be \(S\)-type if the Gram parity pattern persists. Then

\[
X_3J-JX_3
\]

is \(D\)-type, not fixed-sector.

If \(X_3\) has an unexpected \(D\)-component, then this becomes a new row-table issue. It is not the same \(X_{1,S}X_{2,D}\) channel.

### 4.5 \(K_5\), \(K_3\), pure rows

Under the current septic/nonic sector ledger:

\[
K_5\text{ line terms}\in \mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]

\[
K_3\text{ shadows project away},
\]

and pure same-point odd-germ rows vanish or are not in the same transport carrier.

These can affect full vector bookkeeping, but they do not share the \(K_1\)-transport label

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}.
\]

---

## 5. What exact cancellation would require

A cancellation of

\[
-2yB(X_2)_D I
\]

can happen only in one of three ways.

### 5.1 Incidence

\[
y=0,
\qquad
B=0,
\qquad
(X_2)_D=0.
\]

Equivalently,

\[
y=0,
\qquad
q''=0,
\qquad
\Gamma_{G,2}=0.
\]

These are the explicit order-9 incidence walls.

### 5.2 Same-carrier cancellation

The total skew \(K_1\) carrier may be

\[
Y_{\rm total}=y_{\rm total}J
\]

with

\[
y_{\rm total}=0.
\]

This is still the same incidence wall, now written for the total \(Y\)-coefficient.

### 5.3 New analytic cross-channel identity

A different row could cancel this one only if the draft proves an identity of the form

\[
R_9^{I}
=
+2yB(X_2)_D I
\]

with \(R_9^I\) coming from a different channel.

Such an identity would be a genuine row-table theorem, not a consequence of matrix-sector algebra. No such identity is currently known.

So the precise statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{sector/parity algebra does not force cancellation of }Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}.
}
\]

---

## 6. Independence theorem

\[
\boxed{
\begin{minipage}{0.9\linewidth}
At \(g=h\), the row
\[
T_9^{(1,2)}
=
X_{-,1}YX_{+,2}+X_{-,2}YX_{+,1}
\]
has the unique formal channel label
\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}.
\]
Its fixed-sector projection is
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}T_9^{(1,2)}
=
-2yB(X_2)_D I.
\]
No direct \(N_9\), \(X_1N_7X_1\), \(X_2N_7X_0\), \(K_5\)-line, \(K_3\)-shadow,
or pure same-point row has the same channel labels.  Therefore this row is
independent at the channel/sector level.  Its vanishing requires one of the
incidence conditions
\[
y=0,\qquad q''=0,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}=0,
\]
or a new explicit same-order analytic row identity.
\end{minipage}
}
\]

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The order-9 row }Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}\text{ is channel-independent modulo explicit incidence.}
}
\]

Thus the local order-9 theorem is conditionally viable:

\[
\boxed{
g=h,\quad y\ne0,\quad q''\ne0,\quad \Gamma_{G,2}\ne0
\Longrightarrow
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\ne0
}
\]

provided the remaining row-table audit does not discover a new analytic identity cancelling the candidate.

The order-9 route is a genuine improvement over order 7: it replaces dependence on first Gram anisotropy

\[
g-h
\]

with second Gram anisotropy

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}
=
(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}.
\]

It does not remove the eventual quantitative-separation problem, but it gives a real fixed-sector escape from the \(g=h\) wall.
