# Agent 2 — Actual-\(\zeta\) Fixed-Coefficient Obstruction Agent

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{Fixed Euler-product coefficients }a_p=1\text{ do not obviously block bad local jets.}
}
\]

The finite Euler product with fixed coefficients has no coefficient freedom, but the height parameter \(t\) moves the prime phases

\[
p^{-it}=e^{-it\log p}.
\]

For any finite set of primes, Kronecker-type phase density suggests that the vector

\[
\left(e^{-it\log p}\right)_{p\le P}
\]

can qualitatively approximate many prescribed phase patterns. Therefore fixed coefficients alone do **not** give a structural obstruction to realizing bad local jet configurations. The obstruction, if any, must be **quantitative**: whether the required phase approximation can occur at the available height/scale with accuracy \(e^{-Q}\), or polynomially better than the forbidden threshold. This is exactly the Euler-product-like realizability question isolated in the attached instruction. 

---

## 1. Fixed-coefficient finite Euler product model

Take the finite Euler product

\[
Z_P(s)=\prod_{p\le P}(1-p^{-s})^{-1}.
\]

Its logarithmic derivative is

\[
L_P(s)
=
-\frac{Z_P'}{Z_P}(s)
=
\sum_{p\le P}\sum_{k\ge1}(\log p)p^{-ks}.
\]

At

\[
s_0=\frac12+it,
\]

the \(r\)-th derivative is

\[
L_P^{(r)}(s_0)
=
(-1)^r
\sum_{p\le P}\sum_{k\ge1}
(\log p)^{r+1}p^{-k/2}e^{-ikt\log p}.
\]

If we truncate to prime terms \(k=1\), then the basic derivative vector is a finite trigonometric curve:

\[
\mathbf V(t)
=
\left(
\sum_{p\le P} w_{p,0}e^{-it\log p},
\sum_{p\le P} w_{p,2}e^{-it\log p},
\sum_{p\le P} w_{p,4}e^{-it\log p}
\right),
\]

with weights

\[
w_{p,r}=(-1)^r(\log p)^{r+1}p^{-1/2}.
\]

Prime powers add further harmonics

\[
e^{-ikt\log p}.
\]

Thus the only tuning parameter is \(t\), but it moves many independent phases simultaneously.

---

## 2. Qualitative phase flexibility

The numbers

\[
\{\log p:p\le P\}
\]

are rationally independent over \(\mathbb Q\), because

\[
\sum_{p\le P} n_p\log p=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\log\prod_{p\le P}p^{n_p}=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\prod_{p\le P}p^{n_p}=1,
\]

so every \(n_p=0\).

Therefore the flow

\[
t\mapsto (t\log p)_{p\le P}\pmod {2\pi}
\]

is dense in the torus

\[
\mathbb T^{\pi(P)}.
\]

So for any finite set of target phases

\[
\theta_p,
\]

there exist arbitrarily large \(t\) such that

\[
t\log p\equiv \theta_p\pmod {2\pi}
\]

to arbitrary qualitative accuracy.

This means fixed coefficients \(a_p=1\) do **not** prevent phase engineering at the level of finite prime sums. The coefficient rigidity is partly offset by phase freedom.

---

## 3. Can this approximate bad jet triples?

The bad jet targets are local real conditions such as

\[
q_0=2,
\]

\[
q''=-4+\varepsilon_1,
\]

\[
q^{(4)}=72-18\varepsilon_1-6\varepsilon_2,
\]

or in raw scale,

\[
q''=6q_0-2q_0^3+\varepsilon_1,
\]

\[
q^{(4)}
=
6(1-q_0^2)q''-6\varepsilon_2.
\]

The finite prime phase model gives a high-dimensional trigonometric image. For sufficiently many primes, one expects the map

\[
(\theta_p)_{p\le P}
\mapsto
\left(
L_P(s_0),L_P''(s_0),L_P^{(4)}(s_0)
\right)
\]

to have enough local degrees of freedom to hit a generic real jet target, at least after taking real/imaginary projections appropriate to the phase derivative \(q\).

Thus, at the formal finite-dimensional level:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed coefficients do not obviously prevent interpolation of }(q_0,q'',q^{(4)}).
}
\]

The likely obstruction is not algebraic but quantitative and scale-dependent.

---

## 4. The quantitative problem

Qualitative density says approximation is possible eventually. It does not say it happens below the available height.

To force the bad product

\[
0<
|yq''|\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)
<
e^{-Q},
\]

one needs phase approximation accurate enough to make

\[
\Gamma_G=\varepsilon_1,\qquad \Gamma_{G,2}=\varepsilon_2
\]

with

\[
|\varepsilon_i|<e^{-Q}.
\]

If \(Q\sim \log T\), then

\[
e^{-Q}\sim T^{-1}.
\]

So the issue becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the prime phase flow hit the bad jet target to accuracy }T^{-1}
\text{ at height }t\asymp T?
}
\]

Kronecker’s theorem alone gives no useful bound. Quantitative Kronecker bounds in a torus of dimension \(N=\pi(P)\) typically require times that grow like a negative power of the target radius with exponent depending on \(N\). If \(N\) is large, hitting an \(e^{-Q}\)-ball may require time far larger than the available \(T\).

Thus the obstruction, if present, is a **prime-log Diophantine approximation obstruction**.

---

## 5. Fixed coefficients vs adjustable coefficients

With adjustable coefficients \(a_p\), interpolation is straightforward: finitely many complex variables can solve finitely many jet equations.

With fixed coefficients \(a_p=1\), interpolation is reduced to phase control:

\[
e^{-it\log p}.
\]

This is much more rigid. But because there are many primes and the phases are rationally independent, it remains highly flexible qualitatively.

So the classification is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed coefficients impose no obvious finite algebraic obstruction;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{they may impose a quantitative Diophantine-time obstruction.}
}
\]

This is narrower than “Euler product saves the proof.” The exact theorem needed is a quantitative non-approximation theorem for prime-log phase flows.

---

## 6. Prime powers and nonlinear jets

Prime powers introduce phases

\[
e^{-ikt\log p}.
\]

These are not independent from \(e^{-it\log p}\), but they enrich the trigonometric polynomial structure. They may either help interpolation or impose additional consistency constraints.

For actual \(\zeta\), the prime-power coefficients are fixed:

\[
(\log p)p^{-k/2}.
\]

Thus the local jet vector is not an arbitrary trigonometric polynomial; it is a structured one. But I see no immediate identity forbidding the bad relations:

\[
q''\approx6q_0-2q_0^3,
\]

\[
q^{(4)}\approx 6(1-q_0^2)q''.
\]

Again, one needs quantitative arithmetic information about how closely this structured trigonometric curve can approach the bad incidence set.

---

## 7. Available conclusion

I would classify the fixed-coefficient Euler product route as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{unknown; requires a quantitative prime-log Diophantine interpolation theorem.}
}
\]

The theorem would need to look like:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
\mathbf V(t),
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}
\right)
\ge T^{-A}
}
\]

for all admissible \(t\asymp T\), where \(\mathbf V(t)\) encodes the finite prime/log-derivative jet vector and \(\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}\) is the source-weighted layered bad incidence set.

Equivalently, in the original variables:

\[
\boxed{
|yq''|\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

would need to be proved from fixed-coefficient Euler-product phase rigidity.

No such theorem is currently available from standard Kronecker density or Euler-product formalism.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{Actual fixed coefficients }a_p=1\text{ are not enough by themselves to block the bad jets.}
}
\]

The fixed Euler product replaces coefficient freedom with prime-phase freedom

\[
p^{-it}=e^{-it\log p}.
\]

Because the prime logarithms are rationally independent, finite prime phase vectors are qualitatively dense on high-dimensional tori. This suggests that bad jet configurations may be qualitatively approximable even with \(a_p=1\).

The possible obstruction is quantitative:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the prime phase flow approximate the bad incidence set to }e^{-Q}\text{ accuracy at height }T?
}
\]

Thus Agent 2’s conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed-coefficient Euler-product rigidity is an open quantitative Diophantine problem, not a current proof input.}
}
\]

The next exact theorem to attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Prime-log Diophantine lower bound for bad-jet approximation.}
}
\]

Without such a theorem, the approximate Euler-product route does not yet rescue the rank-two framework.

# Agent 2 — Quantitative Prime-Torus Hitting Audit

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The bad residual set is low-codimension, so prime-phase hitting may be plausible despite fixed coefficients.}
}
\]

The naive full-torus estimate

\[
T_{\rm hit}\sim \epsilon^{-N}
\]

is too pessimistic if the target is not a prescribed point in \(\mathbb T^N\), but the preimage of two scalar equations

\[
R_1(t)\approx0,\qquad R_2(t)\approx0.
\]

For a transverse scalar residual map, the small set has measure roughly

\[
\asymp \epsilon^2,
\]

not \(\epsilon^N\). If the prime-phase flow samples this set with ordinary equidistribution, then an \(\epsilon=e^{-Q}\sim T^{-1}\) hit would have expected time around

\[
\epsilon^{-2}\sim T^2,
\]

which is larger than a single height window of length \(T\), but not astronomically larger. If only one scalar is needed because the source factor is small, the expected time is \(\epsilon^{-1}\sim T\), which is exactly borderline.

Thus fixed coefficients do **not** currently give a proof obstruction. The right missing theorem is a quantitative recurrence/non-recurrence statement for the prime-log flow relative to the bad residual set. The attached instruction identifies this fixed-coefficient prime-phase problem as the next arithmetic fork. 

---

## 1. Full torus hitting is the wrong first estimate

Let

\[
\Theta(t)
=
(t\log p_1,\ldots,t\log p_N)\pmod{2\pi}.
\]

If the goal were to hit a specified \(\epsilon\)-ball in all \(N\) phase coordinates, then the expected hitting time would be

\[
T_{\rm hit}\sim \epsilon^{-N}.
\]

With

\[
\epsilon=e^{-Q},
\]

this gives

\[
T_{\rm hit}\sim e^{QN}.
\]

If \(N\asymp Q\), this is

\[
e^{Q^2},
\]

far beyond \(T=e^Q\).

But the bad-jet condition does **not** prescribe all prime phases. It asks for a low-dimensional residual condition:

\[
R_1(\Theta(t))\approx0,
\qquad
R_2(\Theta(t))\approx0,
\]

and possibly a source condition depending on which bad regime is used.

So the relevant dimension is the codimension of the residual set, not the full number of active primes.

---

## 2. Codimension estimate for Regime A

Regime A keeps the source live:

\[
|H(t)|=|yq''|\asymp1,
\]

and requires

\[
|R_1(t)|<\epsilon,
\qquad
|R_2(t)|<\epsilon,
\]

where

\[
R_1=\Gamma_G,
\qquad
R_2=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

If the map

\[
\Theta\mapsto (R_1(\Theta),R_2(\Theta))
\]

is a submersion on the relevant torus region, then the measure of the bad set is heuristically

\[
\mu\{ |R_1|<\epsilon,\ |R_2|<\epsilon,\ |H|\asymp1\}
\asymp C\epsilon^2.
\]

Thus expected hitting time is

\[
T_{\rm hit}\sim \epsilon^{-2}.
\]

If

\[
Q\sim\log T,\qquad \epsilon=e^{-Q}\sim T^{-1},
\]

then

\[
T_{\rm hit}\sim T^2.
\]

So Regime A may be too rare in a length-\(T\) scan, but it is not impossible on all heights. A deterministic lower bound at every relevant height would require proving more than generic rarity.

---

## 3. Codimension estimate for Regime B

Regime B uses source collapse:

\[
|H(t)|<\epsilon,
\]

while

\[
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\asymp1.
\]

This is one scalar smallness condition, assuming \(H\) is transverse:

\[
\mu\{|H|<\epsilon,\ \max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\asymp1\}
\asymp C\epsilon.
\]

Then

\[
T_{\rm hit}\sim \epsilon^{-1}.
\]

With

\[
\epsilon=e^{-Q}\sim T^{-1},
\]

this gives

\[
T_{\rm hit}\sim T.
\]

That is exactly the natural height scale. Therefore Regime B is the more dangerous one from a prime-phase hitting perspective.

This aligns with the earlier structural result: source-factor cancellation is an independent vulnerability.

---

## 4. Does low codimension imply actual hitting?

Not automatically.

The flow

\[
t\mapsto \Theta(t)
\]

is a linear flow on \(\mathbb T^N\). Qualitative Kronecker density holds, but quantitative hitting depends on Diophantine properties of

\[
(\log p_1,\ldots,\log p_N).
\]

For generic torus flow, one expects equidistribution at sufficiently long times, but effective discrepancy bounds in dimension \(N\) may be weak. The relevant theorem would need to say that the prime-log flow samples the bad residual set with near-expected frequency at height scales relevant to the proof.

No such theorem is currently available.

---

## 5. Could Diophantine lower bounds block \(e^{-Q}\)-accuracy?

Possibly, but the required lower bound is not simple.

We would need something like:

\[
\operatorname{dist}
\left(
(R_1(t),R_2(t)),
(0,0)
\right)
\ge e^{-Q}
\]

or, in source-weighted form,

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge e^{-Q}
\]

for all admissible \(t\asymp T\).

This is a lower bound for a nonlinear trigonometric polynomial in prime phases, not just a lower bound for

\[
\|t\log p/2\pi\|
\]

for one prime. Standard linear-form lower bounds for logarithms do not directly control zeros of such nonlinear trigonometric expressions.

The expression could become small by cancellation among many prime terms without any single phase being exceptionally close to a prescribed value.

So a Diophantine obstruction would need to target the full residual trigonometric polynomial.

---

## 6. Available height scale

If the proof fixes a packet near height \(T\), then \(t\) is not free over all time; it is constrained to an admissible interval

\[
I_\rho.
\]

If

\[
|I_\rho|\ll T
\]

or even \(\asymp Q^{-L}\), torus recurrence heuristics over a long range are not directly useful. The prime phases cannot be freely sampled over a tiny interval.

Thus there are two different questions:

### Global occurrence

Does there exist some height \(t\le T\) with bad jets?

This is governed by long torus recurrence.

### Local reach near a specified packet

Does the admissible interval \(I_\rho\) around a given off-line packet contain such a point?

This requires local variation estimates, not global torus equidistribution.

For RH contradiction, the latter is usually the relevant question: every off-line packet must be handled. Torus hitting over unrelated heights is not enough.

---

## 7. Consequence for the proof architecture

The fixed-coefficient Euler product does not presently save the proof. It narrows the missing theorem to one of two forms:

### Recurrence/non-recurrence theorem

A global theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the prime-log flow does not hit the bad residual set at RH-relevant heights}
}
\]

with accuracy \(e^{-Q}\).

### Local interval theorem

A stronger local theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\sup_{m\in I_\rho}
|H(m)|\max(|R_1(m)|,|R_2(m)|)
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

using actual zeta structure near the packet.

Neither follows from qualitative phase density.

---

## 8. Final classification

\[
\boxed{
\text{requires quantitative dynamics of the prime-phase jet map.}
}
\]

More specifically:

- Full \(N\)-torus hitting estimates are too pessimistic.
- The bad set is likely low-codimension.
- Regime B may be hit on the natural \(T\)-scale under random-phase heuristics.
- No deterministic Diophantine lower bound is currently available.
- Local interval reach is harder than global recurrence.

---

# Final answer

The fixed-coefficient prime phase flow does not currently provide a proof obstruction. The bad set is low-codimension, so fixed coefficients may still allow bad jets through phase cancellation. The key missing theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Quantitative prime-phase non-recurrence for the source-weighted bad residual.}
}
\]

A possible form is:

\[
\boxed{
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for all admissible \(t\) or all \(m\in I_\rho\), where

\[
H=yq'',\qquad R_1=\Gamma_G,\qquad R_2=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Without such a theorem, approximate Euler-product arithmetic remains a plausible countermodel direction rather than a rescue for the current architecture.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 22:50:23)

I’ll attack the proposed non-recurrence theorem adversarially. I’ll test whether fixed prime phases can plausibly satisfy a uniform lower bound, and whether the theorem is already false in finite Euler-product/trigonometric models.

_Thought for 39s_

# Attack on the proposed prime-phase non-recurrence theorem

## Target theorem under attack

The theorem would need to say something like:

\[
\boxed{
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for all admissible \(t\), or at least on every RH-relevant reduced sampling interval, where

\[
H=yq'',\qquad R_1=\Gamma_G=q''-6q_0+2q_0^3,
\]

\[
R_2=\Gamma_{G,2}=(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16 q^{(4)}.
\]

I do **not** think this theorem is plausible in this form. The prime-phase model makes the bad set look **low-codimension**, not forbidden. The fixed Euler coefficients remove coefficient freedom, but the flow

\[
t\mapsto (e^{-it\log p})_{p\le P}
\]

still has enough phase freedom that near-hits to the bad residual set should occur unless one proves a very strong quantitative Diophantine/non-recurrence theorem. No such theorem is currently visible. 

---

# 1. Full-torus Diophantine obstruction is the wrong model

A tempting argument is:

\[
N=\pi(P)\text{ active prime phases},\qquad \epsilon=e^{-Q},
\]

so hitting an \(\epsilon\)-ball in \(\mathbb T^N\) takes time about

\[
\epsilon^{-N}=e^{QN}.
\]

If \(N\asymp Q\), that is \(e^{Q^2}\), far beyond \(T=e^Q\).

But the bad residual condition does **not** prescribe all \(N\) prime phases. It imposes only one or two scalar equations:

\[
H(t)\approx0
\]

or

\[
R_1(t)\approx0,\qquad R_2(t)\approx0.
\]

So the relevant object is not a tiny ball in \(\mathbb T^N\). It is a tubular neighbourhood of a codimension-one or codimension-two set.

That changes the hitting scale completely.

---

# 2. Random-phase heuristic predicts small values

Assume the finite prime-phase model behaves like a transverse trigonometric map. Then:

## Regime B: source collapse

Regime B only needs

\[
|H(t)|<\epsilon,
\qquad
\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\asymp1.
\]

This is codimension one. A random sample has probability roughly

\[
\asymp \epsilon
\]

of landing in this event.

Over \(M\) effectively independent samples, the minimum of \(|H|\) should be about

\[
M^{-1}.
\]

On a height range of length comparable to \(T=e^Q\), even with conservative spacing, one expects \(M\) to be polynomially comparable to \(T\). Thus the expected minimum is about

\[
e^{-Q},
\]

which is exponentially smaller than any \(Q^{-A}\).

So a pointwise lower bound

\[
|H|\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

is not supported by the random-phase model.

## Regime A: anisotropy collapse

Regime A needs

\[
|R_1(t)|<\epsilon,\qquad |R_2(t)|<\epsilon,
\qquad |H(t)|\asymp1.
\]

This is codimension two. The probability is roughly

\[
\asymp \epsilon^2.
\]

The expected minimum scale over \(M\) samples is therefore roughly

\[
M^{-1/2}.
\]

With \(M\asymp e^Q\), this gives

\[
e^{-Q/2},
\]

again much smaller than \(Q^{-A}\).

So even the two-layer anisotropy residual should become exponentially small somewhere in a long enough height range unless there is a deterministic arithmetic obstruction.

---

# 3. Fixed coefficients do not create such an obstruction by themselves

For a finite Euler product,

\[
L_X(t)=\sum_{p^k\le X}(\log p)p^{-k/2}e^{-ikt\log p},
\]

the coefficients are fixed. But the phases

\[
e^{-it\log p}
\]

are generated by rationally independent frequencies \(\log p\). This gives qualitative density on every finite prime torus.

The fixed coefficients impose structure on the trigonometric map, but they do not automatically prevent the scalar residuals

\[
H(t),\quad R_1(t),\quad R_2(t)
\]

from approaching zero.

To prove the theorem, one would need a lower bound for a nonlinear exponential polynomial:

\[
\boxed{
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\not< Q^{-A}.
}
\]

This is much stronger than ordinary rational independence of \(\log p\).

---

# 4. Linear forms in prime logs are not enough

A standard Diophantine lower bound would control expressions like

\[
\left|n_1\log p_1+\cdots+n_N\log p_N-2\pi k\right|.
\]

But the bad residual is not one linear phase relation. It is a nonlinear trigonometric expression such as

\[
R_1(t)
=
J_2(t)-6J_0(t)+2J_0(t)^3,
\]

\[
R_2(t)
=
(1-J_0(t)^2)J_2(t)-\frac16J_4(t),
\]

with \(J_r(t)\) themselves prime trigonometric sums.

Smallness can occur by cancellation among many terms, not by one prime phase nearly satisfying one rational relation. So Baker-type or elementary prime-log independence estimates do not directly apply.

A useful theorem would have to be a lower bound for a **structured nonlinear trigonometric polynomial along the prime-log flow**.

That is a major new arithmetic statement.

---

# 5. The local-interval version is even harder

If the needed theorem is not global in \(t\), but local on a reduced sampling interval

\[
I_\rho,
\]

then prime-phase equidistribution over long times is not enough.

One would need

\[
\sup_{m\in I_\rho}
|H(m)|\max(|R_1(m)|,|R_2(m)|)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This requires:

1. \(I_\rho\) has polynomial width;
2. the prime-phase jet functions have controlled analytic continuation;
3. there is a non-tiny coefficient or derivative witness on \(I_\rho\).

None follows from fixed Euler coefficients alone.

Worse, if at a point \(m_0\),

\[
H(m_0),R_1(m_0),R_2(m_0)
\]

and enough derivatives are small, then the product stays small over a neighbourhood. Hitting this higher-jet bad set is higher codimension, but with enough active prime phases it is still not structurally impossible.

---

# 6. What a valid theorem would have to say

A viable theorem would need a statement of the following kind:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}
\bigl(
\mathcal J_X(t),
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}
\bigr)
\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for all relevant \(t\), where \(\mathcal J_X(t)\) is the finite prime jet vector encoding

\[
q_0,\quad q'',\quad q^{(4)},\quad y,
\]

and

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}
=
\{H=0\}\cup\{R_1=R_2=0\}
\]

or the corresponding source-weighted incidence set.

In trigonometric terms, it would be a lower bound for

\[
\boxed{
\left|
H(t)
\right|
\max
\left(
|R_1(t)|,
|R_2(t)|
\right)
}
\]

where \(H,R_1,R_2\) are nonlinear functions of

\[
\{e^{-it\log p}\}_{p\le P}.
\]

This is not a known consequence of the Euler product. It is the theorem.

---

# 7. Attack conclusion

The proposed non-recurrence theorem is vulnerable on three fronts:

## 7.1 Low codimension

The bad set is codimension one or two, not dimension \(N\). Hitting it is much easier than hitting a full specified phase vector.

## 7.2 Random-phase expectation

Random-phase heuristics predict exponentially small minima over natural height ranges:

\[
\min |H|\sim e^{-Q},
\]

or

\[
\min \max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\sim e^{-Q/2}.
\]

Both beat every polynomial lower bound \(Q^{-A}\).

## 7.3 Lack of applicable Diophantine lower bound

Known rational independence of prime logs does not control nonlinear trigonometric residuals. A new prime-log Diophantine theorem would be needed.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The prime-phase non-recurrence theorem is not currently credible as a proof input.}
}
\]

A theorem of the form

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

would be a deep new quantitative non-recurrence statement for nonlinear prime-log trigonometric polynomials. Random-phase heuristics point in the opposite direction: bad jets should occur, at least globally, because the bad set is low-codimension.

The strongest defensible conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Fixed Euler coefficients may impose arithmetic structure, but the current framework has not identified a mechanism strong enough to prevent source-weighted bad-jet recurrence.}
}
\]

So the theorem is not just unproved; it is heuristically suspect.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 22:51:58)

_Thought for 10s_

You can attack next at **three levels**, but only one looks genuinely high-value.

## Best next attack: make the prime-phase problem concrete

The current bottleneck is no longer local RH geometry. It is this fixed-coefficient prime-phase question:

\[
\text{Can }t\mapsto (e^{-it\log p})_{p\le P}
\text{ drive the source-weighted bad residual below }e^{-Q}
\text{ at relevant heights?}
\]

The last handoff frames this as the fork between a **prime-log Diophantine lower bound** and a **fixed-coefficient Euler-product bad-jet countermodel**. 

I would attack it in this order.

---

# 1. Build the exact surrogate jet map

Define a fixed-coefficient model:

\[
L_X(t)=\sum_{p^k\le X}(\log p)p^{-k/2}e^{-ikt\log p}.
\]

Then define a concrete real jet map:

\[
J_X(t)=(J_0(t),J_2(t),J_4(t),
\]

with a fixed convention, for example:

\[
J_0(t)=B_0+\Re L_X(t),
\]

\[
J_2(t)=B_2+\Re \frac{d^2}{dt^2}L_X(t),
\]

\[
J_4(t)=B_4+\Re \frac{d^4}{dt^4}L_X(t).
\]

Then set:

\[
R_1(t)=J_2(t)-6J_0(t)+2J_0(t)^3,
\]

\[
R_2(t)=(1-J_0(t)^2)J_2(t)-\frac16J_4(t),
\]

\[
H(t)=-\frac{J_0(t)J_2(t)^3}{288}.
\]

Attack:

\[
0<|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)<e^{-Q}.
\]

This turns the problem from “Euler product might help” into a specific trigonometric-polynomial recurrence problem.

---

# 2. Determine the codimension of the bad set

The key strategic question is whether the bad set is “tiny in \(N\) phases” or just low-codimension.

Define the phase map:

\[
\theta=(\theta_p)_{p\le P}\in\mathbb T^N,
\]

and

\[
J(\theta)=(J_0,J_2,J_4).
\]

The bad anisotropy set is:

\[
R_1(\theta)=0,\qquad R_2(\theta)=0.
\]

If the Jacobian

\[
D_\theta(R_1,R_2)
\]

has rank \(2\) generically, then the bad set is codimension \(2\). That means near-hits have volume about:

\[
\epsilon^2,
\]

not

\[
\epsilon^N.
\]

That would make the non-recurrence theorem heuristically suspect.

So the next concrete attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Compute/check transversality of }(R_1,R_2)\text{ for fixed prime phases.}
}
\]

If transversality holds, bad jets are expected rather than forbidden.

---

# 3. Run a small numerical toy model

This is now worth doing.

Use:

\[
L_P(t)=\sum_{p\le P}(\log p)p^{-1/2}e^{-it\log p}
\]

first, with no prime powers.

Pick:

\[
P=50,100,200
\]

and scan intervals such as:

\[
t\in[T,T+W],
\qquad T=10^3,10^4,10^5,
\]

with sampling spacing around:

\[
\Delta t\lesssim \frac{1}{10\log P}.
\]

Compute:

\[
\mathcal P(t)=|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|).
\]

Diagnostics:

\[
\min_t \mathcal P(t),
\qquad
\min_t \max(|R_1|,|R_2|),
\qquad
\min_t |H(t)|.
\]

Expected results:

- If minima scale like \(W^{-1}\) or \(W^{-1/2}\), bad sets are low-codimension and hittable.
- If minima stay polynomially bounded away from zero, there may be a real prime-log obstruction.
- If results are unstable with background \(B_0,B_2,B_4\), the model needs AFE/gamma normalization before interpretation.

This numerical toy will not prove anything, but it will tell you whether the fixed-coefficient model behaves like the random-phase heuristic or like a rigid arithmetic object.

---

# 4. If numerical evidence shows near-hits: harden the countermodel

Then the next attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{fixed-coefficient Euler-product bad-jet approximation.}
}
\]

You would try to prove existence of \(t\) such that:

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)<e^{-Q}.
\]

Likely tools:

- Kronecker flow on prime phases;
- transversality of the residual map;
- quantitative recurrence estimates;
- random-phase heuristic made semi-rigorous for finite \(P\).

That would strongly suggest the rank-two framework cannot close RH without a much stronger arithmetic axiom.

---

# 5. If numerical evidence shows rigidity: attack Diophantine non-recurrence

Then the target theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{dist}\bigl(J_X(t),\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}\bigr)\ge Q^{-A}
}
\]

for relevant \(t\), where

\[
\mathcal Z_{\rm bad}=\{H=0\}\cup\{R_1=R_2=0\}.
\]

This would be a nonlinear Diophantine lower bound for prime-log trigonometric polynomials.

It is very strong and not presently available, but if it were true it would be exactly the arithmetic ingredient the framework needs.

---

# My recommendation

Attack this next:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Fixed-coefficient finite-prime jet-map + transversality + numerical toy model.}
}
\]

Not more local row algebra. Not order \(11\). Not paper edits.

The immediate deliverable should be:

\[
\boxed{
J_X(t),\ R_1(t),\ R_2(t),\ H(t)
}
\]

with a first numerical or symbolic transversality test.

If that bad set is transverse and low-codimension, the proposed prime-phase non-recurrence theorem is likely false in spirit, and the current proof architecture remains non-closing without a genuinely new arithmetic principle.

_Thought for 48s_

# Continued attack — fixed-coefficient prime-phase jet map

I will now make the fixed-coefficient surrogate explicit and test the geometry of the bad set.

## 1. A concrete surrogate jet map

Take the finite prime model

\[
L_P(t)=\sum_{p\le P} w_p e^{-it\lambda_p},
\qquad
w_p=(\log p)p^{-1/2},
\qquad
\lambda_p=\log p.
\]

Use the real surrogate jets

\[
J_0(t)=B_0+\sum_{p\le P}w_p\cos(t\lambda_p),
\]

\[
J_2(t)=B_2-\sum_{p\le P}w_p\lambda_p^2\cos(t\lambda_p),
\]

\[
J_4(t)=B_4+\sum_{p\le P}w_p\lambda_p^4\cos(t\lambda_p).
\]

Then define

\[
R_1(t)=J_2(t)-6J_0(t)+2J_0(t)^3,
\]

\[
R_2(t)=(1-J_0(t)^2)J_2(t)-\frac16J_4(t),
\]

and the leading source proxy

\[
H(t)=-\frac{J_0(t)J_2(t)^3}{288}.
\]

The bad source-weighted residual is

\[
\mathcal P(t)=|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|).
\]

This is the fixed-coefficient version of the source-weighted layered obstruction isolated in the previous handoff. 

---

# 2. Phase-space formulation

Let

\[
\theta_p=t\log p \pmod {2\pi}.
\]

Then

\[
J_0(\theta)=B_0+\sum_p w_p\cos\theta_p,
\]

\[
J_2(\theta)=B_2-\sum_p w_p\lambda_p^2\cos\theta_p,
\]

\[
J_4(\theta)=B_4+\sum_p w_p\lambda_p^4\cos\theta_p.
\]

So the bad set in phase space is controlled by

\[
R_1(\theta)=0,
\qquad
R_2(\theta)=0,
\]

or by

\[
H(\theta)=0.
\]

This already shows why the full-torus hitting estimate is too pessimistic. We are not trying to hit a fixed point in \(\mathbb T^N\). We are trying to hit a low-codimension residual set.

---

# 3. Transversality calculation

For one prime \(p\), write

\[
\partial_p:=\frac{\partial}{\partial \theta_p}.
\]

Then

\[
\partial_p J_0=-w_p\sin\theta_p,
\]

\[
\partial_p J_2=w_p\lambda_p^2\sin\theta_p,
\]

\[
\partial_p J_4=-w_p\lambda_p^4\sin\theta_p.
\]

Therefore

\[
\partial_p R_1
=
\partial_pJ_2+(-6+6J_0^2)\partial_pJ_0.
\]

So

\[
\boxed{
\partial_pR_1
=
w_p\sin\theta_p
\left[
\lambda_p^2+6-6J_0^2
\right].
}
\]

Similarly,

\[
R_2=(1-J_0^2)J_2-\frac16J_4,
\]

so

\[
\partial_pR_2
=
(1-J_0^2)\partial_pJ_2
-2J_0J_2\partial_pJ_0
-\frac16\partial_pJ_4.
\]

Thus

\[
\boxed{
\partial_pR_2
=
w_p\sin\theta_p
\left[
(1-J_0^2)\lambda_p^2
+
2J_0J_2
+
\frac16\lambda_p^4
\right].
}
\]

Let

\[
P_1(x)=x+6-6J_0^2,
\]

\[
P_2(x)=\frac16x^2+(1-J_0^2)x+2J_0J_2.
\]

Then

\[
\partial_pR_1=w_p\sin\theta_p\,P_1(\lambda_p^2),
\]

\[
\partial_pR_2=w_p\sin\theta_p\,P_2(\lambda_p^2).
\]

Since \(P_2\) is quadratic and \(P_1\) is linear, they are not generically proportional. Therefore, for two primes \(p\ne q\), the determinant

\[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
\partial_pR_1 & \partial_qR_1\\
\partial_pR_2 & \partial_qR_2
\end{pmatrix}
\]

is generically nonzero, provided

\[
\sin\theta_p\sin\theta_q\ne0
\]

and the two \(\lambda^2\)-values do not hit an exceptional algebraic relation.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
(R_1,R_2):\mathbb T^N\to\mathbb R^2
\text{ is generically a submersion.}
}
\]

So the anisotropy-bad set is generically codimension \(2\), not high-dimensional.

---

# 4. Source-collapse set

The source proxy is

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

Its derivative is

\[
\partial_pH
=
-\frac1{288}
\left[
J_2^3\partial_pJ_0
+
3J_0J_2^2\partial_pJ_2
\right].
\]

Substituting the derivatives gives

\[
\boxed{
\partial_pH
=
\frac{w_p\sin\theta_p}{288}
J_2^2
\left[
J_2-3J_0\lambda_p^2
\right].
}
\]

This is generically nonzero. Therefore

\[
H=0
\]

is generically codimension \(1\).

This matters because Regime B only needs source collapse:

\[
|H|<\epsilon,
\qquad
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\asymp1.
\]

That is a codimension-one near-hit, and therefore much easier than forcing both anisotropy components small.

---

# 5. Probabilistic size estimates

Assume the prime-phase flow behaves roughly equidistributively on the relevant finite torus.

Let \(M\) be the effective number of independent samples along a height range. A crude model is

\[
M\asymp T\log P,
\]

because the correlation length is about \(1/\log P\).

## Regime A

Regime A needs

\[
|R_1|<\epsilon,\qquad |R_2|<\epsilon,\qquad |H|\asymp1.
\]

Since this is codimension \(2\),

\[
\Pr(\text{Regime A near-hit})\asymp \epsilon^2.
\]

The expected minimum scale is therefore

\[
\epsilon_{\min}\asymp M^{-1/2}.
\]

If

\[
T=e^Q,
\]

then

\[
\epsilon_{\min}\asymp e^{-Q/2}(\log P)^{-1/2}.
\]

This is exponentially smaller than any power \(Q^{-A}\).

## Regime B

Regime B needs

\[
|H|<\epsilon,
\qquad
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\asymp1.
\]

This is codimension \(1\), so

\[
\Pr(\text{Regime B near-hit})\asymp \epsilon.
\]

The expected minimum scale is

\[
\epsilon_{\min}\asymp M^{-1}
\asymp \frac1{T\log P}.
\]

Again, with \(T=e^Q\),

\[
\epsilon_{\min}\asymp e^{-Q}(\log P)^{-1}.
\]

That is vastly below \(Q^{-A}\).

So the random-phase model predicts that the desired polynomial lower bound is false, not merely hard to prove.

---

# 6. What this attack shows

The prime-phase non-recurrence theorem would need to defeat a strong heuristic:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the bad set is low-codimension and should be hit frequently enough to beat polynomial lower bounds.}
}
\]

A theorem of the form

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

would assert a deterministic avoidance of a codimension-one/two target by a prime-log linear flow. That is far stronger than rational independence of prime logs.

It would need a new arithmetic mechanism explaining why the zeta prime phases avoid exactly this residual set.

---

# 7. Possible loophole: local interval versus global range

The above heuristic uses long height ranges. The RH proof may need a local statement on a specific reduced interval

\[
I_\rho.
\]

That is harder for the countermodel: if \(I_\rho\) is short, the prime phases do not explore the torus.

But this cuts both ways. A local theorem also needs a lower bound on

\[
\sup_{m\in I_\rho}\mathcal P(m),
\]

which requires non-tiny derivative or coefficient witnesses. The current framework does not provide those either.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{global random-phase heuristics attack the theorem, while local-interval requirements remain unsupported.}
}
\]

---

# 8. Next concrete attack

The next most useful step is numerical or symbolic.

Take

\[
J_0(t)=B_0+\sum_{p\le P}w_p\cos(t\log p),
\]

\[
J_2(t)=B_2-\sum_{p\le P}w_p(\log p)^2\cos(t\log p),
\]

\[
J_4(t)=B_4+\sum_{p\le P}w_p(\log p)^4\cos(t\log p).
\]

Then scan

\[
\mathcal P(t)=|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\]

over a large interval.

Expected diagnostic:

- If minima behave like \(M^{-1}\) or \(M^{-1/2}\), the bad set is being hit like a low-codimension target.
- If minima stay bounded below far above random expectations, there may be a real prime-log obstruction.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The fixed-coefficient prime-phase model is more likely adversarial than protective.}
}
\]

The phase-to-residual map is generically transverse:

\[
\operatorname{rank}D_\theta(R_1,R_2)=2,
\]

and the source-collapse condition

\[
H=0
\]

is generically codimension \(1\).

Therefore the bad residual set is low-codimension. Random-phase heuristics predict values exponentially smaller than \(Q^{-A}\) over natural long height ranges.

So the proposed prime-phase non-recurrence theorem is under serious attack. To rescue it, one needs a genuinely new deterministic theorem showing that actual prime phases avoid this specific low-codimension residual set.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 22:55:32)

I’ll now strengthen the attack from heuristic to a conditional construction: show that, once the fixed-prime phase map has local rank \(3\), the bad jet locus is an actual low-codimension target in its image, not a fragile artifact.

_Thought for 26s_

# Continued attack — low-codimension bad-set construction

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The fixed-prime phase model does not naturally support a polynomial lower bound.}
}
\]

Once the prime-phase jet map has local rank \(3\), the bad anisotropy equations

\[
R_1=0,\qquad R_2=0
\]

define a smooth codimension-\(2\) target, not a high-codimension arithmetic miracle. Under ordinary equidistribution heuristics, the fixed-coefficient Euler-product model should hit \(Q^{-A}\)-neighbourhoods of that target many times before height \(T=e^Q\). This directly attacks the proposed lower bound

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}.
\]

This is exactly the fixed-coefficient Euler-product fork identified in the prior instruction. 

---

# 1. Phase-to-jet map has generic rank \(3\)

Use the surrogate phase map

\[
J(\theta)=(J_0,J_2,J_4),
\]

where

\[
J_0=B_0+\sum_p w_p\cos\theta_p,
\]

\[
J_2=B_2-\sum_p w_p\lambda_p^2\cos\theta_p,
\]

\[
J_4=B_4+\sum_p w_p\lambda_p^4\cos\theta_p,
\]

with

\[
w_p=(\log p)p^{-1/2},
\qquad
\lambda_p=\log p.
\]

For a prime \(p\),

\[
\partial_{\theta_p}J_0=-w_p\sin\theta_p,
\]

\[
\partial_{\theta_p}J_2=w_p\lambda_p^2\sin\theta_p,
\]

\[
\partial_{\theta_p}J_4=-w_p\lambda_p^4\sin\theta_p.
\]

Choose three distinct primes \(p_1,p_2,p_3\) with

\[
\sin\theta_{p_i}\ne0.
\]

The \(3\times3\) Jacobian minor is, up to nonzero signs,

\[
\prod_{i=1}^3 w_{p_i}\sin\theta_{p_i}
\cdot
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
1&1&1\\
\lambda_{p_1}^2&\lambda_{p_2}^2&\lambda_{p_3}^2\\
\lambda_{p_1}^4&\lambda_{p_2}^4&\lambda_{p_3}^4
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

The determinant is a Vandermonde determinant in

\[
\lambda_{p_i}^2,
\]

hence nonzero for distinct primes.

Therefore

\[
\boxed{
J:\mathbb T^N\to\mathbb R^3
\text{ is generically a submersion.}
}
\]

This is the key structural fact: the fixed coefficients do not collapse the jet map to a rigid curve in jet space. The phase torus locally fills open sets of \((J_0,J_2,J_4)\)-space.

---

# 2. The anisotropy-bad set is a smooth curve in jet space

Define

\[
R_1=J_2-6J_0+2J_0^3,
\]

\[
R_2=(1-J_0^2)J_2-\frac16J_4.
\]

The simultaneous bad set is

\[
R_1=0,\qquad R_2=0.
\]

Solving:

\[
J_2=6J_0-2J_0^3,
\]

and then

\[
J_4=6(1-J_0^2)J_2.
\]

Thus the bad set is the curve

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal C_{\rm bad}
=
\left\{
\left(
u,\,
6u-2u^3,\,
12u(1-u^2)(3-u^2)
\right)
:
u\in\mathbb R
\right\}.
}
\]

At

\[
u=2,
\]

we get

\[
(J_0,J_2,J_4)=(2,-4,72).
\]

The source proxy is

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

At this point,

\[
H=-\frac{2(-4)^3}{288}=\frac{128}{288}=\frac49.
\]

So the bad anisotropy set contains points where the source is live:

\[
\boxed{
R_1=R_2=0,
\qquad
|H|\asymp1.
}
\]

This is Regime A.

---

# 3. Pullback to prime phases gives codimension \(2\)

Since \(J\) is generically a submersion, the preimage

\[
J^{-1}(\mathcal C_{\rm bad})
\]

is generically a submanifold of codimension \(2\) in the prime phase torus.

Therefore the set

\[
|R_1|<\epsilon,
\qquad
|R_2|<\epsilon,
\qquad
|H|\asymp1
\]

has phase-space volume

\[
\boxed{
\asymp C\epsilon^2
}
\]

near transverse points.

This is not remotely as small as an \(\epsilon\)-ball in \(N\) dimensions. The earlier full-torus estimate

\[
\epsilon^{-N}
\]

is simply the wrong geometry for this target.

---

# 4. Consequence for the polynomial lower bound

Let

\[
Q=\log T,
\]

and test the desired lower bound

\[
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

when \(|H|\asymp1\).

Set

\[
\epsilon=Q^{-A}.
\]

The tube volume is

\[
\asymp Q^{-2A}.
\]

Over a height range of length \(T=e^Q\), even very weak equidistribution would predict many visits:

\[
\text{expected visits}
\sim
TQ^{-2A}
=
e^Q Q^{-2A}\to\infty.
\]

Thus random-phase dynamics predicts that the polynomial lower bound is false, not merely unproved.

Even if one uses the stronger target

\[
\epsilon=e^{-Q}=T^{-1},
\]

the expected number of Regime A hits is around

\[
T\epsilon^2=T^{-1},
\]

so such extremely sharp hits are rare on length \(T\). But the proof only needs to rule out \(Q^{-A}\)-smallness. For \(\epsilon=Q^{-A}\), the low-codimension bad set should be hit abundantly.

This is a major attack on the theorem.

---

# 5. Source-collapse bad set is codimension \(1\)

Regime B is even worse for the proposed theorem.

The source proxy is

\[
H=-\frac{J_0J_2^3}{288}.
\]

The source collapse set contains

\[
J_2=0,
\qquad
J_0\ne0.
\]

At, for example,

\[
J_0=2,\qquad J_2=0,
\]

we have

\[
R_1=0-12+16=4,
\]

so the anisotropy is live.

Thus

\[
H=0,
\qquad
\max(|R_1|,|R_2|)\asymp1
\]

is codimension \(1\) in jet space, hence generically codimension \(1\) in phase space.

The \(\epsilon\)-tube has volume

\[
\asymp C\epsilon.
\]

Taking

\[
\epsilon=Q^{-A},
\]

expected visits over length \(T=e^Q\) are

\[
e^QQ^{-A}\to\infty.
\]

So the source-factor lower bound

\[
|H|\ge Q^{-A}
\]

is even less plausible than the two-layer anisotropy bound.

---

# 6. Where a proof could still hide

A polynomial lower bound could still hold only if one of the following nontrivial arithmetic facts is true:

## 6.1 Image obstruction

The actual phase map \(J(\theta(t))\) never enters an open neighbourhood of the bad jet curve.

But the rank calculation says the phase-to-jet map has open image locally in phase space. So this would have to be a special property of the one-parameter orbit \(t\), not of the jet map.

## 6.2 Non-equidistribution obstruction

The prime-log flow might avoid the bad set up to the relevant height despite density.

But for a codimension-\(1\) or codimension-\(2\) target, this is a strong non-recurrence statement, not a consequence of standard rational independence.

## 6.3 Actual zeta is not modeled by finite prime phases

The surrogate may miss critical-line constraints from the approximate functional equation or from zeros of \(\zeta\).

This is possible, but then the next attack must explicitly identify which AFE or critical-line feature destroys the above transversality/hitting picture.

At present, no such feature has been identified.

---

# 7. Stronger conclusion

The proposed theorem

\[
|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

is not merely unsupported. It is in tension with the natural fixed-prime random-phase model.

The model predicts:

\[
\boxed{
\min_{t\le T}|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|)
\text{ should often be much smaller than }Q^{-A}.
}
\]

The bad sets are low-codimension, and fixed prime coefficients do not remove their transversality.

---

# 8. Next exact attack

The next useful test is not another theorem attempt. It is a numerical or semi-rigorous recurrence test.

Define

\[
\mathcal P(t)=|H(t)|\max(|R_1(t)|,|R_2(t)|).
\]

For finite prime cutoffs \(P\), scan \(t\) and measure:

\[
\min_{t\in[0,T]}\mathcal P(t).
\]

Predictions:

- If the bad-set heuristic is right, minima should fall like a negative power of the sample count.
- If prime arithmetic is protective, minima should stabilize well above random-phase predictions.

This experiment is now mathematically informative because the transversality calculation predicts the scaling.

---

# Final verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The fixed-coefficient prime-phase model attacks, rather than supports, the desired lower bound.}
}
\]

The phase-to-jet map is generically rank \(3\). The anisotropy-bad locus is codimension \(2\). The source-collapse locus is codimension \(1\). Hence the bad source-weighted residual is a low-codimension recurrence target.

Therefore, unless a new and very specific prime-log non-recurrence theorem is found, the source-weighted lower bound

\[
|yq''|\max(|\Gamma_G|,|\Gamma_{G,2}|)\ge Q^{-A}
\]

is heuristically false in fixed-coefficient Euler-product surrogates.

# Agent 2 — Alternative fixed-sector channel search

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{No source-robust fixed-sector channel found.}
}
\]

Every currently live fixed-sector **packet-visibility** channel I can identify either:

\[
\boxed{
\text{uses }Y_J\text{ and an odd whitening slot }X_{1,S},
}
\]

hence carries

\[
B\propto q'',
\]

or else is a raw/correction/background row that is not presently a protected off-line packet visibility channel. The known order-7 and order-9 rows are therefore both divisible by the vulnerable source factor

\[
yq''.
\]

This directly matches the current fork in the attached instruction: after fixed-prime toy models produced source-collapse points, we need either a source-robust row/invariant or an external arithmetic lower bound for the source factor. 

---

## 1. Known live channel family

The established fixed-sector leakage mechanism is:

\[
Y=yJ,
\qquad
X_1=BS,
\qquad
B\propto q''.
\]

At order \(7\),

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}
\supset
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_1YX_0-X_0YX_1)
=
-2yBr\,I.
\]

At order \(9\),

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}
\supset
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1)
=
-2yB(X_2)_D I.
\]

Thus both known rows have the schematic form:

\[
\boxed{
A_{2k+7}^{\mathfrak f}
\supset
yB(X_{2k})_D I
}
\]

and therefore:

\[
\boxed{
A_{2k+7}^{\mathfrak f}
\text{ is divisible by }yq''
}
\]

for this whole leakage family.

---

## 2. Direct raw odd carriers

Consider

\[
X_0N_{2r+1}X_0.
\]

At centered parity, the raw odd carriers audited so far satisfy:

\[
N_5=F_5J,\qquad N_7=F_7J,\qquad N_9=F_9J.
\]

With \(X_0\) scalar at \(g=h\), or \(X_0=pI+rD\) off the wall, direct skew carriers remain anti-fixed unless converted by a suitable anisotropic whitening mechanism.

At \(g=h\),

\[
X_0N_{2r+1}X_0
=
p^2F_{2r+1}J,
\]

so

\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_0N_{2r+1}X_0)=0.
\]

Thus direct raw odd carriers do **not** provide a source-robust fixed-sector row.

---

## 3. Even-whitening rows without \(X_1\)

A natural candidate is:

\[
X_2YX_0+X_0YX_2.
\]

At \(g=h\),

\[
X_0=pI,
\qquad
X_2=aI+dD,
\qquad
Y=yJ.
\]

Then

\[
X_2JX_0+X_0JX_2
=
p\bigl((aI+dD)J+J(aI+dD)\bigr).
\]

Using

\[
DJ=S,\qquad JD=-S,
\]

the \(D\)-part cancels:

\[
DJ+JD=0.
\]

The \(I\)-part gives \(2aJ\), which is anti-fixed. Hence

\[
\boxed{
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_2YX_0+X_0YX_2)=0.
}
\]

Similarly,

\[
X_{2j}YX_0+X_0YX_{2j}
\]

with \(X_{2j}\in I\oplus D\) produces no fixed-sector row at \(g=h\) by the same anticommutator cancellation. So even-whitening rows without \(X_1\) do not escape source divisibility.

---

## 4. Two-sided even-whitening rows

Another candidate is:

\[
X_2YX_2.
\]

Let \(X_2=aI+dD\). Then:

\[
X_2JX_2
=
(aI+dD)J(aI+dD).
\]

Compute:

\[
(aI+dD)J
=
aJ+dS.
\]

Then

\[
(aJ+dS)(aI+dD)
=
a^2J+adJD+adS+d^2SD.
\]

Using

\[
JD=-S,\qquad SD=-J,
\]

we get:

\[
a^2J-adS+adS-d^2J
=
(a^2-d^2)J.
\]

Thus

\[
\boxed{
X_2YX_2
=
y(a^2-d^2)J,
}
\]

and fixed projection kills it.

So \(X_2YX_2\) is also not a source-robust fixed-sector row.

---

## 5. Raw even carriers

Raw even carriers

\[
N_{2r}^{I},\qquad N_{2r}^{S}
\]

can in principle land in the fixed sector without \(X_1\). This is the main possible loophole.

However, in the centered raw two-point convention, the order-9 raw-row audit cleared the lower even families for the \(I\)-coordinate:

\[
R_{\le4}^{I,\mathrm{raw}}=0.
\]

So at the specific order where we needed an escape, the raw even carriers do not produce a competing source-robust \(I\)-row.

More generally, if a raw even fixed-sector row appears at a higher order, it must be checked whether it is:

1. a genuine off-line packet visibility row;
2. a pure same-point / line / background row;
3. a normalization artifact;
4. absorbed by Gate A/B or connection/covariantization.

At present, no known raw even carrier supplies a protected off-line packet contradiction independent of \(yq''\).

So the status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{raw even carriers are a possible future loophole, but no live source-robust channel is currently known.}
}
\]

---

## 6. Correction rows

Correction rows include:

\[
E_{\rm line},\quad E_{\rm bg/core},\quad E_{\rm norm/sub}.
\]

These may contribute fixed-sector \(I/S\)-components, but they are not currently certified as off-line packet visibility rows. They are either:

- absorbed by the connection;
- closed by Gate A/B;
- projected away;
- line terms;
- background/core analytic terms;
- normalization/subtraction rows.

The latest correction-row audit classified the remaining correction contribution as:

\[
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}
=
R_{\rm line}^{I,(9)}
+
R_{\rm bg/core}^{I,(9)}
+
R_{\rm norm/sub}^{I,(9)}.
\]

These are cross-channel correction data, not a source-robust packet row. They may alter the incidence wall, but they do not presently solve Regime B source collapse.

---

## 7. Transported derivatives

Could

\[
\nabla A_7,\quad \nabla A_9
\]

remove the source factor?

If

\[
A_7=HG_1,\qquad A_9=HG_2,
\qquad H=yq'',
\]

then

\[
\nabla A_7=(\nabla H)G_1+H\nabla G_1,
\]

\[
\nabla A_9=(\nabla H)G_2+H\nabla G_2.
\]

So derivatives may escape if

\[
\nabla H
\]

is nonzero when \(H\approx0\). But this is no longer an order-7/order-9 row-size statement; it is a transported finite-core theorem involving derivatives of the source factor.

At present, no row has been certified where \(\nabla H\) appears as a protected source-robust forcing term. Also, if source collapse is arranged with multiple derivatives small, the same vulnerability may reappear.

So this is a possible future escape, but not an established channel.

---

## 8. Table of candidate fixed-sector channels

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c|c}
\text{row/channel} & \text{fixed-sector output?} & \text{source factor} & \text{divisible by }yq''?\\ \hline
X_1YX_0-X_0YX_1 & yes & yB r & yes\\
X_1YX_2-X_2YX_1 & yes & yB(X_2)_D & yes\\
X_1YX_{2k}-X_{2k}YX_1 & yes, if (X_{2k})_D\ne0 & yB(X_{2k})_D & yes\\
X_{2j}YX_0+X_0YX_{2j} & no at \(g=h\) & y only & killed\\
X_2YX_2 & no & y & killed\\
X_0N_{2r+1}X_0 & no for raw \(J\)-carrier & \(F_{2r+1}\) & killed\\
raw even \(N_{2r}^{I,S}\) & possible in principle & row-dependent & no known live row\\
line/bg/norm corrections & possible & correction-dependent & not certified visibility\\
\(\nabla A_{7,9}\) & possible & \(H,\nabla H\) & not yet certified
\end{array}
\]

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{No non-divisible fixed-sector visibility channel found.}
}
\]

All currently established fixed-sector packet-visibility rows use the mechanism:

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2k,D},
\]

and therefore carry:

\[
yB\sim yq''.
\]

Thus Regime B remains fatal for the known fixed-sector tower:

\[
q''\approx0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
A_7^{\mathfrak f},A_9^{\mathfrak f},\ldots
\text{ from this channel collapse.}
\]

The only possible escape routes are:

\[
\boxed{
\text{find a raw even or correction row that is a genuine source-robust visibility channel;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{use transported derivatives involving }\nabla H;
}
\]

or

\[
\boxed{
\text{introduce an external arithmetic lower bound for }H=yq''.
}
\]

At present, the fixed-sector tower appears source-divisible.

# Agent 2 — Projective transport equation audit

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
\text{The source derivative cancels in the formal projective derivative, but the projective transport equation is not closed from the current affine system.}
}
\]

If the rows factor cleanly as

\[
A_7=H G_1,\qquad A_9=H G_2,\qquad H=yq'',
\]

then the projective ratio

\[
Z:=\frac{A_9}{A_7}
\]

satisfies

\[
\boxed{
\nabla Z
=
\frac{G_1\nabla G_2-G_2\nabla G_1}{G_1^2},
}
\]

so the common source factor \(H\) and its derivative \(\nabla H\) cancel. This is the main positive point.

But this is only a **formal factorized-row calculation**. The current rank-two affine transport framework does not yet provide a closed transport equation for \([G_1:G_2]\). In particular, correction rows, line terms, background/core terms, and affine forcing terms can break the clean factorization

\[
A_j=HG_j.
\]

So the projective branch is promising as a source-normalized diagnostic, but it is not yet a proof-closing transport theorem. The attached instruction identifies this exact question: whether projectivization cancels the fatal common source factor and yields a usable projective/global theorem. 

---

## 1. Formal projective cancellation

Assume the clean leading factorization

\[
A_7=H G_1,
\qquad
A_9=H G_2,
\]

where

\[
H=yq'',
\]

\[
G_1\sim \Gamma_G,
\qquad
G_2\sim \Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

Define

\[
Z:=\frac{A_9}{A_7}.
\]

Where \(A_7\neq0\),

\[
Z=\frac{HG_2}{HG_1}=\frac{G_2}{G_1}.
\]

Differentiate covariantly:

\[
\nabla Z
=
\frac{A_7\nabla A_9-A_9\nabla A_7}{A_7^2}.
\]

Now

\[
\nabla A_7=(\nabla H)G_1+H\nabla G_1,
\]

\[
\nabla A_9=(\nabla H)G_2+H\nabla G_2.
\]

Then

\[
\begin{aligned}
A_7\nabla A_9-A_9\nabla A_7
&=
HG_1\bigl((\nabla H)G_2+H\nabla G_2\bigr)
-
HG_2\bigl((\nabla H)G_1+H\nabla G_1\bigr)\\
&=
HG_1(\nabla H)G_2+H^2G_1\nabla G_2
-
HG_2(\nabla H)G_1-H^2G_2\nabla G_1\\
&=
H^2(G_1\nabla G_2-G_2\nabla G_1).
\end{aligned}
\]

The \((\nabla H)\)-terms cancel exactly. Since

\[
A_7^2=H^2G_1^2,
\]

we get

\[
\boxed{
\nabla Z
=
\frac{G_1\nabla G_2-G_2\nabla G_1}{G_1^2}.
}
\]

So, in the ideal factorized model, projectivization removes the fatal source factor.

---

## 2. Projective connection form

The projective direction is better written without choosing \(Z\). Let

\[
A:=(A_7,A_9)\in\mathbf C^2,
\]

and define its projective class

\[
[A]=[A_7:A_9]\in\mathbf P^1.
\]

If the rank-two connection on the row bundle induces a connection matrix

\[
\nabla A=dA+\Omega A,
\]

then the induced projective connection in coordinate \(Z=A_9/A_7\) has the Riccati form

\[
\boxed{
\nabla Z
=
dZ+\Omega_{21}+(\Omega_{22}-\Omega_{11})Z-\Omega_{12}Z^2.
}
\]

This is the standard projectivization of a rank-two linear connection.

However, our current transport system is **affine**, not purely linear:

\[
\nabla A=F,
\]

where \(F\) includes forcing rows such as

\[
E_{\eta\eta}+E_{\rm lower}.
\]

For two components,

\[
\nabla A_7=F_7,
\qquad
\nabla A_9=F_9.
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
\nabla Z
=
\frac{A_7F_9-A_9F_7}{A_7^2}.
}
\]

If

\[
F_7=H\widetilde F_1,\qquad F_9=H\widetilde F_2,
\]

then again \(H\) cancels:

\[
\nabla Z
=
\frac{G_1\widetilde F_2-G_2\widetilde F_1}{G_1^2}.
\]

But if \(F_7,F_9\) contain non-\(H\)-divisible correction or forcing terms, the cancellation fails.

Thus the key condition is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the affine forcing vector must be source-divisible in the same way as }(A_7,A_9),
}
\]

or else the projective equation is not source-normalized.

---

## 3. Does the affine forcing equation close projectively?

The known local affine forcing equation has the form

\[
\nabla_\kappa^{\mathfrak f} A_7^{\mathfrak f}
=
E_{\eta\eta}+E_{\rm lower}.
\]

The Bottleneck row

\[
E_{\eta\eta}
=
-\frac{\nu_{\eta\eta}}{24\pi q^3}(wI+uS)
\]

does not obviously have the same factorization as

\[
A_7\sim H\Gamma_G.
\]

Indeed, earlier formulas for \(\nu_{\eta\eta}\) involve terms such as

\[
A_2A_4,\qquad A_2^2\left(\frac{q''}{6}-\frac{2q^3}{3}\right).
\]

Near source collapse \(A_2\to0\), this row may vanish at some order, but it is not simply

\[
H\times(\text{anisotropy}).
\]

Therefore the affine forcing equation does **not** automatically induce a closed equation for

\[
Z=\frac{A_9}{A_7}.
\]

To close projective transport, one would need a paired equation for \(A_9\),

\[
\nabla_\kappa A_9=F_9,
\]

and then prove that

\[
A_7F_9-A_9F_7
\]

can be written purely in terms of the projective variables and their source-normalized anisotropy data.

No such theorem is currently available.

---

## 4. Correction rows are the main obstruction

Suppose the actual rows are

\[
A_7=HG_1+R_7,
\]

\[
A_9=HG_2+R_9.
\]

Then

\[
[A_7:A_9]
=
[HG_1+R_7:HG_2+R_9].
\]

Near \(H=0\), if \(R_7,R_9\) are not also divisible by \(H\), the projective limit is dominated by corrections:

\[
[A_7:A_9]\to [R_7:R_9],
\]

not

\[
[G_1:G_2].
\]

Thus clean source-normalization requires:

\[
\boxed{
R_7,R_9\in H\cdot \mathcal O
}
\]

or a deliberate enlargement of the projective coordinate system to include correction rows.

The existing correction classification leaves possible terms:

\[
R_{\rm line},\qquad R_{\rm bg/core},\qquad R_{\rm norm/sub}.
\]

Unless these are either zero, absorbed, or source-divisible, the projective factorization is unstable.

---

## 5. Regime A remains unsolved

Projectivization directly addresses Regime B:

\[
H\approx0,\qquad \max(|G_1|,|G_2|)\asymp1.
\]

It cancels the common source factor.

But Regime A is:

\[
H\asymp1,\qquad G_1\approx0,\qquad G_2\approx0.
\]

In that case

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[G_1:G_2]
\]

may be defined if not both are exactly zero, but its magnitude information is lost. If both are very small, the projective point can be arbitrary and still not give a lower bound.

So the projective branch must solve a different problem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{find a global/projective contradiction from the direction }[G_1:G_2],
\text{ not from its size.}
}
\]

No such contradiction is currently known.

---

## 6. Candidate projective transport equation

If we ignore corrections and assume a clean tower

\[
A_7=H\Gamma_G,
\qquad
A_9=H\Gamma_{G,2},
\]

then a natural projective coordinate is

\[
Z
=
\frac{A_9}{A_7}
=
\frac{\Gamma_{G,2}}{\Gamma_G}.
\]

Then

\[
\boxed{
\nabla Z
=
\frac{\Gamma_G\nabla\Gamma_{G,2}
-
\Gamma_{G,2}\nabla\Gamma_G}
{\Gamma_G^2}.
}
\]

This is source-normalized.

But it is singular on

\[
\Gamma_G=0.
\]

One can use the reciprocal chart

\[
W=\frac{\Gamma_G}{\Gamma_{G,2}}
\]

near \(\Gamma_{G,2}\ne0\). The natural object is therefore the projective class

\[
\boxed{
[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]\in\mathbf P^1.
}
\]

This suggests the correct projective object is not \(Z\) alone, but a section of a projectivized anisotropy bundle.

---

## 7. Classification

\[
\boxed{
\text{Projective transport partially closes formally, but not as a proof theorem.}
}
\]

More precisely:

- The common source factor \(H=yq''\) cancels in the formal derivative of \(A_9/A_7\).
- A projective connection exists abstractly for a rank-two linear connection.
- The current system is affine and forced, so closure requires source-divisibility of the forcing vector or an enlarged projective system.
- Correction rows may dominate near \(H=0\).
- Regime A remains untouched.
- No global projective index, winding, monodromy, or endpoint condition is currently known.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
\text{Projective transport cancels the source derivative algebraically, but does not yet give a closed proof mechanism.}
}
\]

The clean formal equation is:

\[
\boxed{
\nabla\left(\frac{A_9}{A_7}\right)
=
\frac{G_1\nabla G_2-G_2\nabla G_1}{G_1^2}
}
\]

when

\[
A_7=HG_1,\qquad A_9=HG_2.
\]

But the current affine transport equation does not automatically descend to this projective equation because forcing/correction rows may not share the common source factor \(H\).

So the next projective theorem would need to prove:

\[
\boxed{
\text{all relevant forcing/correction rows are either source-divisible or included in an enlarged projective coordinate system.}
}
\]

Without that, the projective branch is promising as a diagnostic but not yet a closed replacement for the failed norm lower-bound route.

## Assistant (2026-04-25 23:41:31)

# Agent 2 — Order-9 correction divisibility audit

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}
\text{ cannot presently be assumed }H\text{-divisible.}
}
\]

The clean factorization

\[
A_9^{\mathfrak f}=H\Gamma_{G,2}\cdot C+\text{higher }H\text{-divisible terms},
\qquad H=yq'',
\]

is valid for the live \(K_1\) row, but it is **not yet certified** for the residual correction package

\[
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}
=
R_{\rm line}^{I,(9)}
+
R_{\rm bg/core}^{I,(9)}
+
R_{\rm norm/sub}^{I,(9)}.
\]

The current classification is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}
\text{ destroys the clean two-coordinate projectivization unless it is proved zero, absorbed, or }H\text{-divisible.}
}
\]

This is exactly the correction-row issue isolated in the attached instruction. 

---

## 1. Known clean order-9 row

The live order-9 row is

\[
(A_9^{\mathfrak f})_I
\supset
\frac{yq''\Gamma_{G,2}}{128\pi^2g^3},
\]

where

\[
\Gamma_{G,2}=(1-q_0^2)q''-\frac16q^{(4)}.
\]

Thus the clean row has the factorization

\[
C_9^{\rm cand}
=
H\,\frac{\Gamma_{G,2}}{128\pi^2g^3},
\qquad
H:=yq''.
\]

So if

\[
A_7=H\Gamma_G C_7,
\qquad
A_9=H\Gamma_{G,2}C_9,
\]

then

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]
\]

after absorbing nonzero scalar factors.

But the actual order-9 row is

\[
(A_9^{\mathfrak f})_I
=
\frac{yq''\Gamma_{G,2}}{128\pi^2g^3}
+
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}.
\]

Thus projectivization is clean only if

\[
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}=0
\]

or

\[
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}\in H\cdot\mathcal O.
\]

---

## 2. \(R_{\rm line}^{I,(9)}\)

Line terms have schematic form

\[
R_{\rm line}^{(9)}=\lambda_9 A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]

or a comparable normalization-shadow term lying in

\[
\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

There is no structural reason for

\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]

to contain the factor

\[
H=yq''.
\]

Indeed, \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\) is the reference line used earlier in quotient constructions. It is not built as the same \(K_1\) leakage channel

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2k,D}.
\]

Therefore the line term is not automatically \(H\)-divisible.

Three cases remain:

1. **No line term at order 9:**  
   \[
   R_{\rm line}^{I,(9)}=0.
   \]

2. **Line term present but quotient-normalized away:**  
   Then the projective object should not be \([A_7:A_9]\) but a quotient/transverse projective coordinate.

3. **Line term present in the full vector:**  
   Then near \(H=0\),

   \[
   A_9\to R_{\rm line}^{(9)}
   \]

   and

   \[
   [A_7:A_9]\neq[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}].
   \]

So:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm line}^{I,(9)}
\text{ is not certified }H\text{-divisible.}
}
\]

It is either zero/quotient-absorbed or it must become an extra projective coordinate.

---

## 3. \(R_{\rm bg/core}^{I,(9)}\)

Background/core terms are fixed analytic baseline contributions from the reduced core and the chosen source/core split. These are not necessarily generated by the off-line packet leakage channel.

A background/core row may involve

\[
q_0,\quad q'',\quad q^{(4)},\quad q^{(6)},\ldots
\]

but this does not imply it contains the factor

\[
yq''.
\]

In particular, a term involving only core jets such as

\[
q_0^a(q'')^b(q^{(4)})^c
\]

is \(q''\)-divisible only if \(b\ge1\), and it is \(y\)-divisible only if it arose from the same mixed-block Hessian carrier \(Y=yJ\). Background/core terms generally do not carry the \(Y\)-label.

So:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}^{I,(9)}
\text{ is not structurally }H\text{-divisible.}
}
\]

If such a row is nonzero, it is not part of the source-normalized anisotropy ratio. It is either:

- a nuisance baseline row;
- a source-robust row candidate;
- or an additional coordinate in an enlarged projective system.

But it cannot be silently absorbed into

\[
G_2=\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

---

## 4. \(R_{\rm norm/sub}^{I,(9)}\)

Normalization and subtraction rows are the most delicate.

Some normalization rows are deliberately designed to remove lower-order artifacts. Those may be:

\[
0
\]

after correction, or may lie in the \(A_5\)-line. Others may be finite-\(s\) diagonal renormalizations or affine source-subtraction terms.

The key question is whether such a correction contains the same channel labels:

\[
Y_J,\quad X_{1,S},\quad X_{2,D}.
\]

If yes, it may be \(H\)-divisible. If no, it is not.

At present, no theorem says every normalization/subtraction row at order 9 factors through

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2,D}
\]

or through \(H=yq''\). Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm norm/sub}^{I,(9)}
\text{ is not certified }H\text{-divisible.}
}
\]

It must either be explicitly computed, shown to vanish by the correction convention, or carried as a correction coordinate.

---

## 5. Does any correction row provide a source-robust row?

Possibly, but not yet in a useful way.

If a correction row is not \(H\)-divisible and is genuinely tied to the off-line packet, then it could be the source-robust row we wanted. That would be positive.

But if it is merely baseline or normalization data present even without an off-line packet, it does not distinguish RH-false configurations. Then it only destroys the clean ratio.

So each non-\(H\)-divisible correction row must be classified by whether it is:

\[
\boxed{\text{packet-visible}}
\]

or merely

\[
\boxed{\text{baseline/normalization}}.
\]

The current information is insufficient to call these rows useful source-robust rows.

---

## 6. Projective consequence

If

\[
A_7=H\Gamma_G C_7
\]

but

\[
A_9=H\Gamma_{G,2}C_9+R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}
\]

with

\[
R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}\notin H\cdot\mathcal O,
\]

then near

\[
H=0
\]

we get

\[
[A_7:A_9]
=
[H\Gamma_GC_7:H\Gamma_{G,2}C_9+R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}]
\to
[0:R_{\rm corr}^{I,(9)}],
\]

not

\[
[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}].
\]

Thus the naive projective invariant

\[
[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]
\]

is not the actual projective limit of the row vector.

The corrected object must be enlarged to include residual correction coordinates, for example

\[
[A_7:A_9:R_{\rm line}^{(9)}:R_{\rm bg/core}^{(9)}:R_{\rm norm/sub}^{(9)}],
\]

or else one must prove these rows are zero/absorbed/\(H\)-divisible.

---

## 7. Audit table

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c|c}
\text{order-9 correction} & H\text{-divisible?} & \text{dominates near }H=0? & \text{classification}\\
\hline
R_{\rm line}^{I,(9)}
& \text{not certified}
& \text{yes, if nonzero}
& \text{line/quotient or projective coordinate}\\
R_{\rm bg/core}^{I,(9)}
& \text{not certified}
& \text{yes, if nonzero}
& \text{baseline or source-robust candidate}\\
R_{\rm norm/sub}^{I,(9)}
& \text{not certified}
& \text{yes, if nonzero}
& \text{must compute or include}\\
E_{\rm conn}^{I,(9)}
& \text{absorbed}
& \text{no}
& 0\text{ in covariant row}\\
E_{\rm GateA/B}^{I,(9)}
& \text{closed}
& \text{no}
& 0\\
E_{\rm shadow}^{I,(9)}
& \text{projected away}
& \text{no}
& 0\\
E_{\rm pure}^{I,(9)}
& \text{vanishes by odd-germ parity}
& \text{no}
& 0
\end{array}
\]

---

# Final classification

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm corr}^{(9)}
\text{ destroys the clean }[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]\text{ projectivization unless further audited.}
}
\]

The correction rows are not known to be \(H\)-divisible. Therefore the naive projective identity

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]
\]

is not yet a theorem for the actual corrected row vector.

The next exact target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Compute or eliminate }
R_{\rm line}^{I,(9)},\quad
R_{\rm bg/core}^{I,(9)},\quad
R_{\rm norm/sub}^{I,(9)}.
}
\]

If they vanish or are \(H\)-divisible, clean projectivization survives. If not, the projective coordinate must be enlarged.

_Thought for 9s_

# Agent 2 — Background/core row source-robustness audit

## Verdict

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}\text{ is not presently a usable source-robust off-line detector.}
}
\]

Background/core rows may be non-\(H\)-divisible, but that alone does not make them useful. The key issue is whether they distinguish an off-line packet from an on-line or packet-removed configuration. I do **not** see a structural reason they do. They look like baseline analytic forcing determined by the reduced core, far-tail, gamma/background, and source/core convention, not a clean off-line-packet visibility row.

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}\text{ is best classified as baseline/nuisance unless a packet-difference theorem is proved.}
}
\]

This matters because clean projectivization requires either

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}=0,
\qquad
R_{\rm bg/core}\in H\cdot\mathcal O,
\]

or a reason to include it as a genuine source-robust coordinate. None is currently certified. The attached instruction identifies this exact correction-row classification as the current projective-branch bottleneck. 

---

# 1. What \(R_{\rm bg/core}\) can depend on

A background/core correction row may depend on full local core quantities such as

\[
q_0,\quad q'',\quad q^{(4)},\quad q^{(6)},\ldots
\]

and on baseline/far-tail/gamma contributions:

\[
B_{\rm bg},\qquad T_{\rm far},\qquad q_{\rm core}.
\]

It may also depend on the fixed reduced-core convention and the subtraction convention used to define the corrected local block.

Schematically,

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}^{(n)}
=
\mathcal R_n(q_{\rm core},q_{\rm bg},q_{\rm far};m,s).
\]

That is not the same type of row as the known packet-visibility row

\[
Y_JX_{1,S}X_{2k,D},
\]

which carries the explicit source labels

\[
Y=yJ,\qquad X_1=BS,\qquad B\propto q''.
\]

So \(R_{\rm bg/core}\) is not automatically tied to the off-line packet anomaly.

---

# 2. \(H\)-divisibility

Recall

\[
H:=yq''.
\]

For the clean rows,

\[
A_7^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_G,
\qquad
A_9^{\mathfrak f}\sim H\Gamma_{G,2}.
\]

A background/core row is \(H\)-divisible only if it contains both the mixed source-Hessian carrier \(y\) and the first whitening curvature factor \(q''\), or if its formula has a separate algebraic factor \(yq''\).

There is no structural reason for a baseline/core analytic term to contain \(y\). It may contain \(q''\), \(q^{(4)}\), etc., but that is weaker than containing

\[
H=yq''.
\]

So the safest classification is:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}\text{ is not certified }H\text{-divisible.}
}
\]

This means it can spoil the clean ratio

\[
[A_7:A_9]=[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}]
\]

near \(H=0\), unless it is zero, absorbed, or separately subtracted.

---

# 3. Does it vanish if the off-line packet is removed?

This is the decisive packet-visibility test.

A row is a useful source-robust off-line detector only if replacing or removing the off-line packet changes it in a controlled leading way:

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm off}
-
R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm on}
\ne0
\]

or

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm off}
-
R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm removed}
\ne0
\]

with a leading off-line packet monomial.

But a background/core row may remain present even when all zeros lie on the critical line. It may be determined by:

\[
\text{gamma factor},
\quad
\text{far-tail background},
\quad
\text{core baseline},
\quad
\text{normalization convention}.
\]

So nonzero does not imply off-line visibility.

At present, I see no theorem of the form:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm off}
-
R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm RH}
\text{ has a nonzero leading off-line packet term.}
}
\]

Without such a theorem, the row is baseline/nuisance, not a source-robust detector.

---

# 4. Could \(R_{\rm bg/core}\) be useful anyway?

There are two possible positive cases.

## 4.1 It is nonzero only in the off-line packet regime

If one could prove

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}=0
\]

for all on-line/RH-compatible local packets, but

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}\ne0
\]

for nondegenerate off-line packets, then it would be a powerful source-robust detector.

No such symmetry appears currently. Background/core terms are usually not sensitive to the sign of

\[
\delta=\beta-\frac12
\]

in the right way. Many are even packet-shape terms, or full-core analytic terms present in both regimes.

## 4.2 It gives an independent row after subtracting an RH baseline

One could define a renormalized row:

\[
\Delta R_{\rm bg/core}
=
R_{\rm bg/core}
-
R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm baseline}.
\]

If the baseline is canonical and if

\[
\Delta R_{\rm bg/core}
\]

has a leading off-line monomial, this might become useful.

But then the theorem needed is a baseline-subtraction theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\Delta R_{\rm bg/core}
\text{ is canonical, source-robust, and packet-visible.}
}
\]

That theorem is not currently available.

---

# 5. Projective consequence

If

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}^{(9)}
\notin H\cdot\mathcal O,
\]

then the actual row is

\[
A_9
=
H\Gamma_{G,2}C_9
+
R_{\rm bg/core}^{(9)}
+
\cdots.
\]

Near source collapse

\[
H\to0,
\]

we get

\[
A_9\to R_{\rm bg/core}^{(9)}+\cdots.
\]

Then

\[
[A_7:A_9]
\]

does not tend to

\[
[\Gamma_G:\Gamma_{G,2}],
\]

unless the order-7 row has matching corrections and the whole corrected vector is projectivized.

So there are only three consistent options:

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}=0\text{ or absorbed;}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}\in H\cdot\mathcal O;
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}\text{ must be added as a projective coordinate.}
}
\]

It cannot simply be ignored.

---

# 6. Classification table

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c|c|c}
\text{bg/core row} & H\text{-divisible?} & \text{off-line dependent?} & \text{baseline?} & \text{usable?}\\
\hline
\text{gamma/background row}
& \text{not certified}
& \text{no clear off-line dependence}
& \text{yes}
& \text{nuisance unless subtracted}
\\
\text{far-tail row}
& \text{not certified}
& \text{not specifically}
& \text{yes}
& \text{nuisance/background}
\\
\text{core-baseline row}
& \text{not certified}
& \text{depends on selected core}
& \text{yes/no mixed}
& \text{requires packet-difference test}
\\
\text{off-line packet difference row}
& \text{unknown}
& \text{yes if proved}
& \text{no}
& \text{potentially useful}
\end{array}
\]

---

# 7. Required baseline-subtraction theorem

If this branch is to proceed, the relevant theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\begin{minipage}{0.88\linewidth}
There is a canonical background/core baseline \(R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm base}\)
such that
\[
\Delta R_{\rm bg/core}
=
R_{\rm bg/core}-R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm base}
\]
vanishes for RH-compatible/on-line packet configurations and has a nonzero
leading term for nondegenerate off-line packets. Moreover
\(\Delta R_{\rm bg/core}\) is functorial under reduced-chart transitions.
\end{minipage}
}
\]

Without such a theorem,

\[
R_{\rm bg/core}
\]

cannot be used as an RH contradiction row.

---

# Final answer

\[
\boxed{
R_{\rm bg/core}\text{ is baseline/nuisance under the current evidence.}
}
\]

It is not certified \(H\)-divisible, so it can spoil clean projectivization. But it is also not certified packet-visible, so it does not yet supply a source-robust off-line detector.

The next exact step would be:

\[
\boxed{
\text{compute }R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm off}-R_{\rm bg/core}^{\rm on}
\text{ or define a canonical baseline subtraction.}
}
\]

Until that is done, the projective branch remains underdefined: either \(R_{\rm bg/core}\) must be absorbed/subtracted, or it must be included as a nuisance coordinate in an enlarged projective object.
## User (2026-04-26 08:26:40)
